The Mu'tazilite Reliance on Dualist Principles
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite adoption of Dualist principles and their analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi exposes the Mu'tazilite borrowing from the Dualists and refutes their analogy from the witnessed world.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites borrowed core principles from Dualists but contradicted themselves on evil entities.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla uncovers a historical dependency: the Mu'tazilites adopted two key principles from Dualist sects—that creating evil is evil and that an act devoid of benefit is foolishness. However, Nasafi shows how they diverged on the application, particularly regarding evil entities, and how their reliance on analogy from the witnessed world ultimately refutes them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the historical claim and argument flow.
Notice the mention of Dualists and the two principles.
2
Identify the two shared principles
Find where the Mu'tazilites agree with the Dualists: creating evil is evil, and an act devoid of benefit is foolishness.
3
Analyze the point of divergence
See how the Mu'tazilites disagree with Dualists on evil entities and benefit, leading to internal inconsistency.
Consider the role of analogy from the witnessed world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما تلقنوا هذا من إخوانهم الثنوية فبنوا المذهب على قواعدهم ونسجوا على منوالهم، فزعموا أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وأن ما خلا عن النفع فهو سفه وتعلقوا بالشاهد، غير أن الثنوية يزعمون أن ما لا منفعة فيه للفاعل فهو قبيح، ولهذا زعموا أن كل فعل للنور فهو في نفع نفسه هو وخلاصه عن وثائق الظلمة، فصوبت المعتزلة الثنوية في هاتين القاعدتين وزعمت أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وهو سفه، غير أنهم خالفوا الثنوية في تخليق الأجسام المستخبثة المستقذرة والأعيان الضارة، فزعمت الثنوية أنها لما كانت قبيحة كان إيجادها قبيحا وهي محدثة فلابد لها من صانع آخر، وأنكرت المعتزلة قبحها مع شهادة الله تعالى بخبثها وتشبيه الكفر الخبيث بها بقوله: ﴿ وَمَثَلُ كَلِمَةٍ خَبِيثَةٍ ﴾ (سورة إبراهيم - 14 - الآية 26) وزعمت أن القردة والخنازير وإبليس - عليهم اللعنة - ومردة الشياطين كلهم أشياء مستحسنة لا خبث فيها ولا قبح، وإنما القبح في الأفعال، فأنكروا أن يكون الله تعالى خالقًا لها، ولهذا أنكروا حرمة الأعيان النازلة من حرمة الأفعال منزلة الحفظ من الحماية لما لا حرمة إلا بوصف القبح ولا قبح في الأعيان لما أنها لو كانت قبيحة لم يكن إيجاها حكمة، وكذا خالفوا الثنوية في تعليق الحكمة بنفع الفاعل نفسه بل علقوها بمطلق النفع إما للفاعل وإما لغيره، وتمسك الفريقان - أعني المعتزلة والثنوية - في الأمرين جميعًا أعني - أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وأن الفعل الخالي عن النفع سفه – بالشاهد، فنوقضت المعتزلة بما بينا أن الباري يرى عبده يزني بأمته ويخلى بينهما مع القدرة
And they learned this only from their brothers the Dualists, so they built the doctrine on their principles and wove on their pattern. They claimed that creating evil is evil and that what is devoid of benefit is foolishness, and they clung to the witnessed. However, the Dualists claim that what has no benefit for the agent is evil, and for that reason they claimed that every action of the light is for its own benefit and its liberation from the bonds of darkness. So the Mu'tazilites approved of the Dualists in these two principles and claimed that creating evil is evil and it is foolishness. But they disagreed with the Dualists regarding the creation of impure, filthy bodies and harmful entities. The Dualists claimed that since they are evil, their creation is evil, and they are originated, so they must have another creator. The Mu'tazilites denied their evil despite God's testimony of their impurity and His likening evil unbelief to them in His saying: {And the example of a bad word} (Surah Ibrahim 14:26), and they claimed that monkeys, pigs, Iblis—curses be upon them—and the rebellious devils are all commendable things with no impurity or evil in them, and that evil is only in actions. So they denied that God is their Creator, and for this they denied the prohibition of entities descending from the prohibition of actions in the station of preservation from protection, because there is no prohibition except by the description of evil, and there is no evil in entities, because if they were evil, their creation would not be wisdom. Similarly, they disagreed with the Dualists in attaching wisdom to the benefit of the agent himself, but rather attached it to absolute benefit, either for the agent or for another. And both groups—namely the Mu'tazilites and the Dualists—held fast in both matters, i.e., that creating evil is evil and that an act devoid of benefit is foolishness, to the witnessed. So the Mu'tazilites were refuted by what we have shown: that the Creator sees His slave committing adultery with his slave-girl and leaves them alone with [His] power...
Source pages p. 926

Commentary
This passage is a historical and doctrinal refutation of the Mu'tazilite position by exposing its origin. Nasafi states that the Mu'tazilites learned their principles from the Dualists (al-Thanawiyya, possibly Manichaeans or Zoroastrians). The Dualists held that creating evil is evil and that an act devoid of benefit for the agent is foolishness. The Mu'tazilites agreed on these two principles but diverged on whether certain entities (e.g., impure bodies) are evil. While Dualists argued that such entities are evil and must have a separate creator, the Mu'tazilites denied their evil, even using a Quranic verse to liken evil speech to evil entities, yet they claimed that animals and devils are commendable, confining evil to actions. They thus denied that God creates evil entities, which leads to denying divine prohibition of such entities. They also disagreed with Dualists on the criterion of benefit: Dualists said benefit must be for the agent himself, Mu'tazilites said benefit could be for the agent or another. Both groups, however, based their arguments on analogy from the witnessed world. Nasafi then points out that the Mu'tazilites are refuted by the earlier example: God allows a slave to commit adultery despite having power to prevent it, which is not considered evil in the unseen, thus breaking their analogy.

Key Terms
الثنوية
المعتزلة
إيجاد القبيح
النفع
الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite view is purely rational and original.
Correction: The passage shows their principles are historically dependent on Dualist premises, not purely rational.

Checkpoint
What two principles did the Mu'tazilites adopt from the Dualists according to Nasafi?
A) That God creates all actions and that benefit is only for the agent.
B) That creating evil is evil and that an act devoid of benefit is foolishness.
C) That evil entities have a separate creator and that benefit must be for the agent.
D) That good and evil are known by reason alone and that God must act for a purpose.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites' principles are not original but borrowed, and their reliance on analogy from the witnessed world fails when applied to the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever uncritically accepted a belief or principle because it seemed reasonable, only to later realize its source was flawed? How can you guard against borrowing ideas that contradict revelation?
?
When you encounter a creature or entity you consider harmful or evil, do you attribute its existence to God's wisdom or to some other cause? How does this passage challenge your default assumption?
?
If your reasoning about God leads you to conclusions that contradict clear Quranic statements—like the impurity of certain beings—are you willing to abandon your reasoning? What is at stake if you don't?

Mu'tazila adopt Dualist principles on evil and benefit
Nasafi recounts Mu'tazila borrowing Dualist principles on evil and benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila adopted two key principles from the Dualists and where they diverged.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila borrowed Dualist ideas but reshaped them to fit their own theology.

In this passage, al-Nasafi explains that the Mu'tazila derived their positions on evil and benefit from the Dualist (Majus) tradition. They agreed that creating evil is itself evil and that an action without any benefit is foolish, but they modified the scope of benefit and denied that physical objects can be intrinsically evil. This sets the stage for subsequent refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold before you interpret.
Notice how Nasafi first states the borrowing, then the two principles, then the differences.
2
Identify the two principles
The passage states two principles the Mu'tazila accepted from the Dualists: that bringing about evil is evil, and that an action devoid of benefit is foolishness (safah).
3
Spot the differences
The Mu'tazila disagreed on two points: they denied that created entities (like pigs, Iblis) are inherently evil, and they broadened benefit to include benefit for others, not just the agent.
This shows they did not accept Dualist teachings wholesale.
4
Note the method of argument
Both groups relied on analogy with the witnessed world (al-shahid) to support their claims. This is a key methodological point.
Understanding this helps see why later refutations focus on the limits of analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما تلقنوا هذا من إخوانهم الثنوية فبنوا المذهب على قواعدهم ونسجوا على منوالهم، فزعموا أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وأن ما خلا عن النفع فهو سفه وتعلقوا بالشاهد، غير أن الثنوية يزعمون أن ما لا منفعة فيه للفاعل فهو قبيح، ولهذا زعموا أن كل فعل للنور فهو في نفع نفسه هو وخلاصه عن وثائق الظلمة، فصوبت المعتزلة الثنوية في هاتين القاعدتين وزعمت أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وهو سفه، غير أنهم خالفوا الثنوية في تخليق الأجسام المستخبثة المستقذرة والأعيان الضارة، فزعمت الثنوية أنها لما كانت قبيحة كان إيجادها قبيحا وهي محدثة فلابد لها من صانع آخر، وأنكرت المعتزلة قبحها مع شهادة الله تعالى بخبثها وتشبيه الكفر الخبيث بها بقوله: ﴿ وَمَثَلُ كَلِمَةٍ خَبِيثَةٍ ﴾ وزعمت أن القردة والخنازير وإبليس عليهم اللعنة ومردة الشياطين كلهم أشياء مستحسنة لا خبث فيها ولا قبح، وإنما القبح في الأفعال، فأنكروا أن يكون الله تعالى خالقًا لها، ولهذا أنكروا حرمة الأعيان النازلة من حرمة الأفعال منزلة الحفظ من الحماية لما لا حرمة إلا بوصف القبح ولا قبح في الأعيان لما أنها لو كانت قبيحة لم يكن إيجاها حكمة، وكذا خالفوا الثنوية في تعليق الحكمة بنفع الفاعل نفسه بل علقوها بمطلق النفع إما للفاعل وإما لغيره، وتمسك الفريقان أعني المعتزلة والثنوية في الأمرين جميعًا أعني أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وأن الفعل الخالي عن النفع سفه بالشاهد.
And they only learned this from their brothers the Dualists, so they built the school on their principles and wove on their loom, and claimed that bringing about evil is evil and that what lacks benefit is foolishness, and they clung to the witnessed world. But the Dualists claim that what has no benefit for the agent is evil, and for this they claimed that every act of light is for its own benefit and its deliverance from the bonds of darkness. So the Mu'tazila approved the Dualists in these two principles and claimed that bringing about evil is evil and it is foolishness, but they differed from the Dualists regarding the creation of loathsome, filthy bodies and harmful entities. The Dualists claimed that since they are evil, bringing them about is evil, and they are originated, so they must have another maker. The Mu'tazila denied their evil despite God's testimony of their foulness and His likening foul unbelief to them in His saying: 'And the example of a bad word' (Ibrahim 26). And they claimed that monkeys, pigs, Iblis—curses upon them—and the rebellious devils are all commendable things with no foulness or evil in them, and evil is only in actions. So they denied that God is their Creator, and for this they denied the prohibition of entities, transferring the prohibition from actions to the level of preservation from protection, because there is no prohibition except by the attribute of evil, and there is no evil in entities because if they were evil, bringing them about would not be wisdom. And likewise they differed from the Dualists in attaching wisdom to the benefit of the agent himself, but rather attached it to absolute benefit, either for the agent or for others. And both groups—meaning the Mu'tazila and the Dualists—clung to the witnessed world in both matters, namely that bringing about evil is evil and that action devoid of benefit is foolishness.
Source pages p. 926

Commentary
This passage describes how the Mu'tazila adopted two key principles from the Dualists (Thaniyya): that creating evil is itself evil, and that an action completely devoid of benefit is foolish (safah). The author first notes that the Dualists specify benefit as being for the agent himself, citing their example that every act of light (the good principle) is for its own benefit. The Mu'tazila agreed on the two principles but differed on two points: first, they denied that certain created entities (like pigs, monkeys, Iblis) are inherently evil, arguing instead that evil lies only in actions; second, they broadened the scope of benefit to include benefit for others, not just the agent. Both groups, however, based their arguments on analogy with the witnessed world (al-shahid), i.e., human experience. The passage sets up the Mu'tazili position that will be refuted subsequently.

Key Terms
الثنوية
المعتزلة
القبيح
النفع
السفه
الشاهد
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila and Dualists agreed on everything regarding evil and benefit.
Correction: They agreed on the two principles but differed on whether entities can be evil (Mu'tazila said no) and on the scope of benefit (Mu'tazila included benefit for others).

Checkpoint
What did the Mu'tazila accept from the Dualists?
A) That creating evil is evil and action devoid of benefit is foolish
B) That all created entities are evil
C) That benefit must be for the agent alone
D) That God creates evil actions

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila adopted the Dualist principles of evil and benefit but modified them to exclude inherent evil in objects and to include benefit for others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world, do you also judge God's actions based on your own sense of benefit? Have you ever wondered why there is evil in creation? What does this lesson tell you about trusting God's wisdom even when you don't see the benefit?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected that God creates evil entities because they thought it would be foolish. Do you ever find yourself questioning God's wisdom when you face hardship or see something you dislike? What would change in your life if you truly believed that everything God creates has a purpose, even if you cannot see it?
?
The Dualists thought light acts only for its own benefit. Do you ever act only for your own gain, even in worship? How does this lesson challenge you to seek benefit for others as well?

The Objection of Benefit and the Invalidation of the Mu'tazilite Analogy
Nasafi presents a dialectical objection against the Mu'tazila, using their own method of analogy to force a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite reliance on analogy with the witnessed world leads to a theological dilemma regarding God's wisdom.

Core Idea
If God is like us, He must benefit Himself; but He does not.

Nasafi presents a powerful objection that turns the Mu'tazilites' own method against them. If they insist that all actions are evaluated by the standard of the witnessed world, then they must conclude that God is not wise—or that He is needy. This dilemma shows the limits of anthropomorphic analogy in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify the claim that actions are only good if they benefit the agent, as the Dualists held.
The objection uses the Mu'tazilite's own principle of analogy (shahid).
2
See the dilemma
Notice how the objection forces the Mu'tazila into either denying God's wisdom or attributing need to Him.
3
Understand the invalidation
The Mu'tazila cannot escape without abandoning their own analogy. If they reject both options, they invalidate their method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل لهم: ما تنكرون على من يقول لا يحسن الفعل في الشاهد إلا لنفع نفسه كما زعمت الثنوية، ومن نفع غيره إنما حسن فعله لأن من نفع غيره نفع نفسه في الحقيقة إنما بنيل الثواب، وإما باكتساب الجاه، وإما بإحراز المحمدة والثناء الجميل حتى إن من نفع غيره بلا حمد يستحق ولا أجر ينال لا يكون ذلك حكمة، فإن تمسكتم بالشاهد فقولوا: إن الله تعالى ليس بحكيم لما أنه لا ينفع بفعله، وإن قلتم: إنه حكيم فقولوا: إنه منتفع محل للحاجات دافع للضرورات، فبأي أمر تمسكوا خرجوا عن الدين، وإن أبوهما أبطلوا استشهادهم بالشاهد، وإذا بطل ذلك فبعد ذلك جمهور متكلمي أهل الحديث يقولون: القبيح ما نهى عنه، والله تعالى ليس بمنهي عن إيجاد الكفر، والعبد منهي عن اكتساب الكفر فكان خلقه تعالى غير قبيح، وكسب العبد قبيحا على هذا يتكلم جمهور متكلمي أهل الحديث، وبعضهم يقولون: القبيح ما يعود به على فاعله الضرر المحض، والله تعالى لا يعود عليه بفعل ما ضرر، فلا يكون خلقه الكفر قبيحًا منه لعدم عود ضرر به عليه، والكافر يعود باكتسابه الكفر عليه ضرر محض فكان اكتسابه الكفر قبيحا، ويجعل الذم والحمد من توابع هذين الفعلين فنقول: إن الفعل الذي يستحق عليه الحمد ما لا يعود به ضرر على فاعله، والذي
And it was said to them: What do you deny of one who says that an action in the witnessed world is only good for the benefit of oneself, as the Dualists claimed, and that whoever benefits others, his action is only good because whoever benefits others benefits himself in reality either by obtaining reward, or by acquiring prestige, or by attaining praise and beautiful commendation, such that if one benefits others without deserving praise or receiving any reward, that is not wisdom. So if you cling to the witnessed world, say that God is not wise because He does not benefit by His action. And if you say that He is wise, then say that He is benefited, subject to needs, repeller of necessities. Whichever thing they cling to, they leave the religion. And if they refuse both, they invalidate their own citation of the witnessed world. And when that is invalidated, then after that the majority of theologians of the People of Hadith say: Evil is that which is forbidden. God is not forbidden to bring about unbelief, while the servant is forbidden to acquire unbelief, so His creation (of unbelief) is not evil, and the servant's acquisition (of unbelief) is evil. This is how the majority of theologians of the People of Hadith speak. And some of them say: Evil is that which returns upon its agent pure harm. God does not suffer any harm from an action, so His creation of unbelief is not evil from Him due to the absence of harm returning upon Him. The disbeliever, by acquiring unbelief, returns upon himself pure harm, so his acquisition of unbelief is evil. And they make blame and praise follow from these two actions. So we say: The action for which one deserves praise is that which does not return harm upon its agent, and which...
Source pages p. 927

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical objection against the Mu'tazila and then states the Sunni position. The objection is: If you insist on analogy with the witnessed world, then either you say benefit is only for the agent (as Dualists hold) or you open the door to including rewards, prestige, etc. Either way, God neither benefits Himself nor receives reward, so He would not be wise. If you reject both, you abandon your own analogy. After invalidating the Mu'tazila position, the author presents the Sunni definition: The majority (jumhur) of theologians of Ahl al-Hadith define evil (qabih) as 'what is forbidden' (ma nuha 'anhu). Since God is not forbidden to create unbelief, His creation is not evil; the servant's acquisition is evil because he is forbidden. An alternative definition among them is that evil is 'what returns pure harm upon its agent'; God suffers no harm, so His act is not evil. Thus blame and praise attach accordingly. The text cuts off at 'and which', likely continuing the explanation.

Key Terms
النفع
الحكمة
الشاهد
الضرر
الثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Mu'tazilite use of analogy is logically sound and can be consistently applied to God.
Correction: This passage shows that the analogy leads to an absurd dilemma: either God is not wise or He is needy. Thus, analogy with the witnessed world is insufficient for understanding divine attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what happens if the Mu'tazila insist on analogy with the witnessed world?
A) They must affirm that God is wise.
B) They must conclude that God is not wise or that He is needy.
C) They can consistently maintain their position.
D) They must reject the concept of wisdom altogether.

Key Takeaway
Analogizing God to the witnessed world inevitably leads to contradictions; the Mu'tazilite method is self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought you understood God by comparing Him to yourself? What dangerous assumptions might that lead to, and how does this lesson expose that? Commit to one area where you will stop using human analogies for God.
?
If you catch yourself thinking 'God wouldn't do that because I wouldn't', what does this passage demand you do about that thought? Write down a specific habit of comparison you must abandon starting today.
?
This argument shows that analogizing God leads to contradictions. What specific comparison do you need to abandon today to protect your tawhid? Be honest—name it and decide how you will replace that thought with awe of God's transcendence.

The Sunni Definition of Evil: Divine Command and Absence of Harm
Nasafi states the Sunni definitions of evil (qabih) as given by the theologians of Ahl al-Hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Sunni definitions of evil (qabih) and how they preserve God's transcendence.

Core Idea
Evil is defined by divine command and harm to the agent, not by God's action.

After refuting the Mu'tazilite approach, Nasafi presents the positive Sunni position. The majority of Ahl al-Hadith theologians define evil either as 'what is forbidden' or as 'what returns pure harm upon its agent'. Both definitions shield God from being the doer of evil while affirming moral accountability for humans.

Guided Path
1
First definition: evil as forbidden
Evil is what God forbids. God is not forbidden, so His creation of unbelief is not evil. The servant's acquisition is evil because he is forbidden.
This definition grounds evil in divine command, not in the nature of the act itself.
2
Second definition: evil as harmful
Evil is what returns pure harm upon the agent. God suffers no harm, so His actions are not evil. The disbeliever harms himself by acquiring unbelief.
This definition uses harm as the criterion, but applies it only to creatures, not to God.
3
Conclusion: blame and praise
Blame and praise follow from these definitions. The action that does not harm the agent deserves praise; that which harms deserves blame.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل لهم: ما تنكرون على من يقول لا يحسن الفعل في الشاهد إلا لنفع نفسه كما زعمت الثنوية، ومن نفع غيره إنما حسن فعله لأن من نفع غيره نفع نفسه في الحقيقة إنما بنيل الثواب، وإما باكتساب الجاه، وإما بإحراز المحمدة والثناء الجميل حتى إن من نفع غيره بلا حمد يستحق ولا أجر ينال لا يكون ذلك حكمة، فإن تمسكتم بالشاهد فقولوا: إن الله تعالى ليس بحكيم لما أنه لا ينفع بفعله، وإن قلتم: إنه حكيم فقولوا: إنه منتفع محل للحاجات دافع للضرورات، فبأي أمر تمسكوا خرجوا عن الدين، وإن أبوهما أبطلوا استشهادهم بالشاهد، وإذا بطل ذلك فبعد ذلك جمهور متكلمي أهل الحديث يقولون: القبيح ما نهى عنه، والله تعالى ليس بمنهي عن إيجاد الكفر، والعبد منهي عن اكتساب الكفر فكان خلقه تعالى غير قبيح، وكسب العبد قبيحا على هذا يتكلم جمهور متكلمي أهل الحديث، وبعضهم يقولون: القبيح ما يعود به على فاعله الضرر المحض، والله تعالى لا يعود عليه بفعل ما ضرر، فلا يكون خلقه الكفر قبيحًا منه لعدم عود ضرر به عليه، والكافر يعود باكتسابه الكفر عليه ضرر محض فكان اكتسابه الكفر قبيحا، ويجعل الذم والحمد من توابع هذين الفعلين فنقول: إن الفعل الذي يستحق عليه الحمد ما لا يعود به ضرر على فاعله، والذي
And it was said to them: What do you deny of one who says that an action in the witnessed world is only good for the benefit of oneself, as the Dualists claimed, and that whoever benefits others, his action is only good because whoever benefits others benefits himself in reality either by obtaining reward, or by acquiring prestige, or by attaining praise and beautiful commendation, such that if one benefits others without deserving praise or receiving any reward, that is not wisdom. So if you cling to the witnessed world, say that God is not wise because He does not benefit by His action. And if you say that He is wise, then say that He is benefited, subject to needs, repeller of necessities. Whichever thing they cling to, they leave the religion. And if they refuse both, they invalidate their own citation of the witnessed world. And when that is invalidated, then after that the majority of theologians of the People of Hadith say: Evil is that which is forbidden. God is not forbidden to bring about unbelief, while the servant is forbidden to acquire unbelief, so His creation (of unbelief) is not evil, and the servant's acquisition (of unbelief) is evil. This is how the majority of theologians of the People of Hadith speak. And some of them say: Evil is that which returns upon its agent pure harm. God does not suffer any harm from an action, so His creation of unbelief is not evil from Him due to the absence of harm returning upon Him. The disbeliever, by acquiring unbelief, returns upon himself pure harm, so his acquisition of unbelief is evil. And they make blame and praise follow from these two actions. So we say: The action for which one deserves praise is that which does not return harm upon its agent, and which...
Source pages p. 927

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical objection against the Mu'tazila and then states the Sunni position. The objection is: If you insist on analogy with the witnessed world, then either you say benefit is only for the agent (as Dualists hold) or you open the door to including rewards, prestige, etc. Either way, God neither benefits Himself nor receives reward, so He would not be wise. If you reject both, you abandon your own analogy. After invalidating the Mu'tazila position, the author presents the Sunni definition: The majority (jumhur) of theologians of Ahl al-Hadith define evil (qabih) as 'what is forbidden' (ma nuha 'anhu). Since God is not forbidden to create unbelief, His creation is not evil; the servant's acquisition is evil because he is forbidden. An alternative definition among them is that evil is 'what returns pure harm upon its agent'; God suffers no harm, so His act is not evil. Thus blame and praise attach accordingly. The text cuts off at 'and which', likely continuing the explanation.

الكسب
الخلق
الضرر
الحمد
الذم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Sunni definition of evil is arbitrary, simply saying 'evil is what God forbids' without further rationale.
Correction: The passage shows that the definitions are grounded in either divine command or harm, and they serve to protect God from being associated with evil while maintaining moral accountability for humans.

Checkpoint
According to the second definition of evil presented in the passage, why is God's creation of unbelief not evil?
A) Because God is not commanded to avoid it.
B) Because it harms no one.
C) Because it brings benefit to believers.
D) Because God does not suffer harm from it.

Key Takeaway
Sunni theologians define evil in a way that removes any implication of evil from God while preserving human moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If evil is defined by what harms you eternally, why do you persist in sins that you know bring harm? Will you not turn back to Allah before the harm becomes irreversible?
?
The theologians said that good and evil are determined by divine command, not by your desires. In what area of your life are you following your own definition of good instead of God's? What will you change starting today?
?
God creates disbelief in the disbeliever, yet it is not evil from Him because it does not harm Him. Do you hate the sin itself or the sinner? Commit to hating the sin while loving the person, and reflect on how this lesson transforms your view of others' faults.

Why Qiyas Fails for God's Actions: The Harm Argument
Al-Isfara'ini defines harm-based blame and rejects analogy to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari refutation of analogical reasoning from visible to invisible concerning evil, based on the impossibility of harm for God.

Core Idea
Qiyas fails because harm is impossible for God, so evil cannot be attributed to Him.

This lesson delves into a concise yet powerful argument by the Ash'ari scholar Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini. He challenges the use of analogical reasoning (qiyas) from the visible world to God, specifically regarding the attribution of evil. By defining evil as that which causes harm to its doer, he shows that since harm is impossible for God, the analogy collapses, thereby refuting the claims of the Mu'tazila and Dualists.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of blame
Read the opening statement: blame is deserved for acts that bring harm to the doer. This is the foundational premise of the argument.
The definition connects moral blame directly to harm.
2
Identify the two realms
Notice the distinction: in the visible world (al-shahid), harm is conceivable, so evil can exist. In the invisible realm (God), harm is impossible, so evil cannot exist.
This asymmetry is key to the refutation.
3
See why analogy fails
Qiyas al-shahid bi al-ghaib relies on shared attributes. Since harm (the basis of evil) is not shared, the analogy is invalid.
4
Recognize the target
The conclusion invalidates the arguments of the Mu'tazila and Dualists who accuse God of evil. This shows the theological stakes.
The Mu'tazila believed God must do what is best; Dualists posited two gods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستحق عليه الذم ما يعود به ضرر على فاعله، وإليه ذهب أبو إسحاق الإسفرائيني فيمتنع على هذا أيضا قياس الشاهد بالغائب لما أن عود الضرر في الشاهد متصور فيتصور القبيح وفي الغائب ممتنع فيمتنع القبيح في الغائب فيبطل به جميع كلام المعتزلة والثنوية.
Blame is deserved for what brings harm to its doer, and Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini adhered to this. So based on this also, qiyas (analogy) of the visible (al-shahid) by the invisible (al-gha'ib) is impossible, because the return of harm in the visible is conceivable, so evil is conceivable, while in the invisible it is impossible, so evil is impossible in the invisible, thus all the speech of the Mu'tazila and the Dualists is invalidated.
Source pages p. 928

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini, an Ash'ari scholar. He defines blameworthy actions as those that harm the doer. Based on this definition, he argues that qiyas al-shahid bi al-ghaib (analogy from the visible to the invisible) cannot be applied to God, because in the visible world, harm is possible, so evil is conceivable. But in the invisible (God), harm is impossible, so evil cannot be attributed to God. Therefore, any argument that attributes evil to God based on analogy fails, and the claims of the Mu'tazila and Dualists who accuse God of evil are invalidated.

Key Terms
الذم
الضرر
قياس الشاهد بالغائب
القبيح
المعتزلة
الثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that qiyas al-shahid bi al-ghaib is always valid for all attributes.
Correction: This passage shows that the analogy fails when the attribute (harm) is categorically impossible for God. Not all human attributes apply to God.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini's rejection of qiyas al-shahid bi al-ghaib regarding evil?
A) Because evil is defined by harm, and harm is impossible for God.
B) Because the Mu'tazila are always wrong.
C) Because the Quran prohibits analogy.
D) Because the Dualists have no evidence.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of harm for God breaks the analogy from the visible world, thus evil cannot be attributed to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship, do you ever feel God has treated you unfairly? How does al-Isfara'ini's claim that harm is impossible for God confront that feeling? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that nothing God does can harm Him?
?
Think of a time you judged someone's action as 'evil' based on the harm it caused. How do you apply that same standard to God? Are you willing to let go of your human measure of fairness when it comes to His actions? What would that surrender look like in your daily life?
?
This lesson shows that reasoning about God using human analogies can lead to false conclusions. Where in your own faith do you rely on human logic instead of divine revelation? Commit to one area where you will submit to God's transcendence rather than your own reasoning.

God's Creation Is Not Futile: Refuting Dualists
Refutation of dualists: God's creation not futile, analogy invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's creation of countless things with no apparent benefit is not futile, and why analogical reasoning from human needs to God is invalid.

Core Idea
God's actions are not bound by human needs, so they cannot be called futile.

This passage continues the refutation of dualist doctrines. The author argues that God's creation includes countless things that seem useless to us, but that does not make them futile. He then explains why using human analogies to judge God's actions is a logical mistake.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice the two distinct arguments: one about creation of hidden things, one about analogical reasoning.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The passage presents two points: first, that God created many things with no apparent benefit, yet it is not futile. Second, that inference from the visible world is impossible because humans have needs, while God does not.
3
Understand the analogy from the visible world
The author says that in the visible world, acting without benefit is foolish only because the agent has needs. God has no needs, so the analogy fails.
This is a key refutation of dualist claims.
4
Reflect on the implication for God's attributes
Consider how this argument protects God's wisdom and transcendence from being measured by human standards.
Review the key terms: عبث (futility), سفه (foolishness), الاستدلال بالشاهد (inference from the visible).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد سبق القول في إبطال مذاهب الثنوية، أن الله خلق ما لا يحصى كثرة مما لا انتفاع لأحد من خلقه به ولا اطلاع لممتحن عليه من الأجزاء الكامنة في تخوم الأرضين وبواطن الجبال وقعور البحار وهو تعالى يجل عن الانتفاع بشيء، ومع ذلك لم يكن خلق ذلك عبثا، على أن الاستدلال بالشاهد محال أيضا لما أن كل فاعل في الشاهد محل الحاجات والضرورات، فإذا اشتغل بما لا نفع له به وهو محتاج إلى اجتلاب المنافع ودفع المضار فكان اشتغاله به إعراضا عما فيه جلب منفعة له ودفع مضرة، فكان ذلك منه رضا بالنقيصة وتحمل المضرة فكان سفها، والله تعالى يجل عن ذلك فلم يكن فعله لا لنفعه سفها
And the discussion has preceded regarding the refutation of the doctrines of the Dualists, that God created countless things from which no one of His creation benefits and no testing person has awareness of the hidden parts in the depths of the earth, the interiors of mountains, and the bottoms of seas, and He is exalted above benefiting from anything, and despite that, His creation of them was not futile, and also that inference by the visible (al-shahid) is impossible because every agent in the visible is subject to needs and necessities; when he engages in something that has no benefit for him while he is in need of attracting benefits and repelling harms, his engagement with it becomes a turning away from what brings him benefit and repels harm, so that from him is contentment with deficiency and enduring harm, so it is foolishness, and God is exalted above that, so His action not for His benefit is not foolishness.
Source pages p. 928

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the Dualists. The author references a previous discussion invalidating dualist doctrines. He provides two main points: First, God created countless things (like the insides of mountains and sea depths) that bring no apparent benefit to anyone, but that does not make them futile. Second, he argues that using analogy from the visible world (al-shahid) to the invisible is impossible. In the visible world, when a person engages in an act that brings them no benefit while they are in need of benefit, that is considered foolishness because they are turning away from their own interests. But God is free from such needs, so applying this analogy to God would be invalid. Therefore, God's action is not foolishness even if it appears to have no benefit from our limited perspective.

Key Terms

الاستدلال بالشاهد
الحاجات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any action with no apparent benefit is futile or foolish, even if performed by God.
Correction: Futility and foolishness arise only when an agent has needs and neglects them. God is free from needs, so His actions cannot be judged by this standard.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that inference by the visible (al-shahid) is impossible when attributing foolishness to God?
A) Because God does not act at all.
B) Because humans are never foolish.
C) Because every agent in the visible has needs, while God is free from needs.
D) Because the Dualists are always correct.

Key Takeaway
God's actions are not subject to human standards of benefit and harm; thus, what appears useless to us may not be futile in His wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you accused God—in your heart—of being unfair or pointless in a trial you faced, because you could see no benefit? How does this lesson expose the subtle pride in demanding that God's actions make sense to you before you submit?
?
Are there areas of your life where you insist on visible results before you trust God's plan? What would it take for you to believe that His hidden work—like the treasures in the depths of the earth—is more real than what you can see?
?
You say you believe God is not needy. But do you live as if He needs your agreement to validate His actions? If He is truly free of all needs, then your doubts about His wisdom are not just ignorance—they are rebellion. Will you repent today and trust that He is never foolish?

The Three (Four) Logical Consequences for the Opponents
Nasafi delineates the unavoidable logical consequences for the opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi logically forces the opponents' position into a triple dilemma, showing that all options lead to either apostasy, contradiction, or acceptance of the truth.

Core Idea
The opponents' own logic traps them: every escape route ends in defeat.

Having already refuted the opponents' position, Nasafi now turns the screw. He presents the logical choices they must face: either they admit that God becomes evil by creating the disbeliever (which strips them of Islam), or they admit consistency would mean God creates disbelief (which is the truth), or they make claims that lead to contradiction. This passage is a masterclass in reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully to grasp the overall argument. Let the logic sink in before analysing.
Notice that Nasafi says 'three matters' but lists four. This is a textual variant; the logic covers all.
2
Identify the first option
The first possibility: the opponents could admit that God becomes evil by creating the disbeliever (since the disbeliever is more evil than disbelief). Nasafi says this would strip them of religion.
3
Identify the second option
The second possibility: they could deny that God becomes evil, forcing them to accept that the creator of disbelief is not evil, hence God must be the creator of disbelief. This leads them to abandon their doctrine and follow the truth.
4
Identify the third and fourth options
The third and fourth options involve contradictory claims: either the servant creates disbelief but is not evil (which entails disbelieving that the disbeliever is evil), or they claim God is not evil for creating the disbeliever but the servant is evil for creating disbelief, which is a clear contradiction.
These two options lead to either disbelief or contradiction.
5
Reflect on the force of the argument
Think about how Nasafi leaves the opponents with no way out. Each possible response either contradicts their own principles, forces them to accept the truth, or destroys their religion.
This is a powerful example of logical argumentation in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فبعد هذا هم بين أمور ثلاثة إما أن يقولوا : إنه تعالى لما أوجد الكافر الذي هو شر من الكفر صار شريراً ، فينسلخوا به عن الدين، وإما أن يقولوا : إنه لما لم يصر شريراً بخلق ما هو شر من الكفر فخالق الكفر لا يكون شريرا فيكون هو الذي خلقه لأنه لا يصير شريراً بخلقه فيصيروا تاركين مذهبهم متبعين للحق، وإما أن يقولوا : خالقه العبد إلا أنه بخلقه الكفر لا يصير شريرًا لما أن خالق الكافر الذي هو شر من الكفر لا يكون شريراً فيكفروا بذلك حيث يزعمون أن الكافر ليس بشرير، وإما أن يزعموا أن الله تعالى بإيجاد الكافر الذي هو شر من الكفر ليس بشرير وموجد الكفر شرير وهو الكافر عندهم، ولو أوجده الله لكان شريرا مع أنه بإيجاد ما هو شر منه ليس بشرير، وهذه مناقضة ظاهرة.
After this, they are faced with three matters: either they say that the Exalted when He brought into existence the disbeliever who is more evil than disbelief, He became evil, so they would thereby strip themselves of religion; or they say that since He did not become evil by creating what is more evil than disbelief, then the creator of disbelief is not evil, so He is the one who created it because He does not become evil by creating it, so they become abandoners of their doctrine, followers of the truth; or they say that the created being is the slave, but by his creating disbelief he does not become evil because the creator of the disbeliever who is more evil than disbelief is not evil, so they thereby become disbelievers, since they claim that the disbeliever is not evil; or they claim that God Almighty by bringing the disbeliever who is more evil than disbelief is not evil, and the one who brings disbelief is evil, and he is the disbeliever according to them, and if God had brought it He would be evil, while He is not evil by bringing what is more evil than it, and this is a clear contradiction.
Source pages p. 932

Commentary
This passage is a logical classification of the consequences for the opponents after the previous refutation. Nasafi outlines four possible positions they might take (though he says 'three'): (1) They could admit that God becomes evil by creating the disbeliever (since the disbeliever is more evil than disbelief), which would lead them out of Islam. (2) They could admit that God does not become evil by creating the greater evil (the disbeliever), so by consistency He would also not become evil by creating the lesser evil (disbelief), thus they would accept that God is the creator of disbelief and abandon their own doctrine, following the truth. (3) They could claim that the servant (human) is the creator of disbelief but that doing so does not make the servant evil, because even the creator of the greater evil (the disbeliever) is not evil. But this leads them to disbelief because it implies the disbeliever is not evil. (4) They could claim that God is not evil for creating the disbeliever (greater evil) yet the one who creates disbelief (the servant) is evil, and if God created disbelief He would be evil — a clear contradiction. Nasafi thus shows that their position is untenable.

Key Terms
كافر
شر
شرير
خلق
مناقضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the opponents have a consistent escape route, but Nasafi shows all options collapse.
Correction: Nasafi demonstrates that every logical possibility leads to either apostasy, contradiction, or acceptance of the truth, leaving no viable alternative.

Checkpoint
What is the first possible position Nasafi says the opponents might take?
A) They admit God becomes evil by creating the disbeliever.
B) They deny God creates anything.
C) They claim the servant is not evil for creating disbelief.
D) They claim God is not evil for creating disbelief.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's logical demolition leaves the opponents with no viable alternative: their position collapses into either apostasy, contradiction, or acceptance of the truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Nasafi forces the opponents to face four impossible outcomes. If you were in their shoes, which of these four would be the hardest to accept? Why? Does your own understanding of God's role in creating good and evil withstand a similar logical test? This week, identify one area where you might hold an inconsistency in your beliefs about God and His actions, and resolve it through study or sincere reflection.
?
The opponents are trapped because they want to avoid attributing evil to God, yet their alternative leads to contradictions. Have you ever felt similar tension between what you believe about God's justice and the evil you see in the world? How should this passage shape your reliance on divine revelation over human reasoning? Commit to reciting the verse 'Have you considered? If it is from Allah, and you disbelieve in it...' (41:52) when you feel that tension.
?
Nasafi's argument shows that denying God's creation of evil logically forces one into apostasy, contradiction, or acceptance of the truth. If you are convinced by this argument, what does it demand of you? Are you willing to submit to God's absolute sovereignty even when it challenges your moral intuitions? Write a short prayer asking Allah to grant you consistency in faith and protect you from logical fallacies that lead to misguidance.

The Ash'ari Criterion for Evil: Transgression of Command
Ash'ari definition of evil as harm plus transgression of command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of evil as harm that involves transgression of a command and how this exonerates Allah from evil.

Core Idea
Evil is defined by transgression, not by harm itself.

The Ash'aris faced a challenge: if Allah creates everything, including disbelief, how can He be free from evil? They answer by distinguishing between harm and transgression. In this passage, the Ash'ari criterion for evil is laid out clearly, using the analogy of a commanding officer. Let's explore how they define evil and why Allah cannot be called evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: a definition, an example, and two applications.
2
Identify the criterion and the example
Find the sentence that states the condition for an act to be evil. Then examine the example of intentional murder versus retributive killing. Note how the harm is the same but the moral judgment differs.
The key phrase is 'مجاوزا أمر آمره' (transgressing the command of his commanding officer).
3
Understand the applications
See how the criterion is applied to Allah and to the human slave. Notice why Allah is not evil despite creating disbelief, and why the slave is evil for acquiring it.
Allah has no superior to transgress, so His actions are never evil by definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأشعرية يقولون : الإضرار متى لم تكن المضر به غیره مجاوزا آمره لا يكون شرا، ومتى كان مجاوزاً أمر آمره كان شراً، ولهذا يقال: من قتل غيره عمدًا إنه ألحق الشر بولده الصغير ولو قتله بقصاص ما كان شريراً ملحقا الشر بولده الصغير، وإن كان الضرر عليه في الحالين سواء، ثم الله تعالى بإيجاده الكفر ما تعدى أمر غيره فلم يكن به شريراً، كما لم يكن بإيجاد الكافر الذي هو شر من الكفر شريراً ، لانعدام تعديه أمر غيره، والعبد باكتسابه جاوز أمر صانعه فكان شريراً.
Then the Ash'aris say: Harm, when the one harmed is not transgressing the command of his commanding officer, is not evil; and when he transgresses the command of his commanding officer, it is evil. For this reason it is said: Whoever kills another intentionally inflicts harm on his young child, but if he kills him as a retribution (qisas), he is not an evil one inflicting harm on his young child, even though the harm in both cases is the same. Then Allah, by His creation of disbelief, did not transgress the command of another, so He is not evil by it, just as He was not evil by creating the disbeliever who is worse than disbelief, due to the absence of his transgression of the command of another. And the slave, by his acquisition (iktisab), transgressed the command of his Maker, so he is evil.
Source pages p. 932

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari criterion for evil. They define evil as harm caused by someone who is transgressing the command of a superior authority. If the harm is done within the bounds of authority, it is not considered evil, even if the harm is identical. The author gives an example: an intentional murderer harms the victim's child, but an executioner who kills by retributive justice (qisas) also harms the child, yet the executioner is not evil because he acts under authority. Similarly, Allah's creation of disbelief is not an act of transgression because there is no superior authority above Him; therefore, He is not evil by creating it. This is consistent with His creating the disbeliever, which is worse, yet He is not evil. In contrast, the slave who acquires (yaktasibu) disbelief is transgressing the command of his Maker, so he becomes evil. Thus, the Ash'aris exonerate God by focusing on the concept of authority and transgression.

Key Terms
إضرار (harm)
مجاوز (transgressing)
آمر (commanding officer)
أمر (command)
قتل (killing)
عمد (intentional)
قصاص (retribution)
اكتساب (acquisition)
صانع (Maker)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any harm caused by God is evil.
Correction: The Ash'aris argue that harm alone is not the criterion; transgression of command is. Since Allah has no superior, His actions are never evil even if they cause harm.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris, what makes an act evil?
A) The amount of harm caused.
B) The intention of the agent.
C) Transgressing the command of a superior authority.
D) The presence of pain.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari criterion for evil is transgression of a superior's command, not the presence of harm itself, which allows them to exonerate Allah from evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you suffer harm, do you instinctively blame God? According to this lesson, what must be true for harm to be evil? How does that change your response to your own trials?
?
Consider a time you accused someone of being evil. Were you assuming they transgressed God's command, or only that they harmed you? How might this criterion reshape your judgment of others?
?
If evil is defined by transgression, and God cannot transgress, then your own sin is not just harm but rebellion. Does that make you more or less aware of your responsibility? What will you do about a specific sin you have been downplaying?

6 min
The Najjāriyya Doctrine of Aspects of Actions
Explanation of Najjāriyya doctrine and first part of kufr example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Najjāriyya claim that actions have multiple aspects and how they apply this to kufr as a thing, accident, and belief.

Core Idea
A single action can be both good and bad from different aspects.

The Najjāriyya school introduced the concept of jihāt al-fi'l to explain how an action can be attributed to both Allah and the servant without contradiction. This lesson presents their foundational claim and the first part of their analysis of kufr, which they argue is not ugly in itself but becomes ugly when considered as the servant's act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Najjāriyya claim
Read the Arabic passage where the author states that the Najjāriyya claim actions have aspects, and that an action can be ugly in one aspect and not in another.
Notice the phrase 'من حيث إنه' which marks each aspect.
2
Identify the aspects of kufr
The passage lists three aspects: kufr as a thing, as an accident, and as a belief. See how the author argues that kufr is not ugly from these aspects.
The reasoning uses a reductio: if kufr were ugly as a thing, then everything, including faith, would be ugly.
3
Understand the logical implication
Ask yourself why the author rejects the idea that kufr is ugly from these aspects. The commentary explains the consistency argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم النجارية على هذا يزعمون أن الفعل له جهات، فمن حيث إنه فعل الله تعالى ليس بقبيح، ومن حيث إنه فعل العبد قبيح، ويجوز أن يكون للفعل جهات تقبح وتكون معصية ببعض الجهات ولا يكون كذلك ببعض الجهات، ألا ترى أن الكفر شيء وهو عرض وهو اعتقاد، وهو حجة الله تعالى على الكافر بالتعذيب وليس بقبيح، فمن حيث إنه شيء إذ لو كان قبيحًا من حيث إنه شيء لكان كل شيء قبيحًا فكان الإيمان قبيحًا سببًا للعقاب، وكذا كل طاعة، وكذا ليس بقبيح من حيث إنه عرض، ولا من حيث إنه اعتقاد لما يلزم أن يكون الإيمان قبيحا منهيًا عنه، وكذا الكفر كذب وهو دليل سفه الكافر، ودلالته على سفهه صدق فكان الكذب صدقا في الدلالة على سفه الكاذب وليس بقبيح ولا سفه لاستحقاق العقاب من حيث إنه صدق بل من حيث إنه كذب. وكذا السواد شيء وعرض ولون، وليس بسواد لأنه شيء ولا لأنه عرض ولا لأنه لون لوجودنا أشياء و أعراضا وألوانا ليست بسواد، ولا يعنون بهذه الجهات كما هو جهات الأجسام من حيث فوق وتحت وعن يمين وعن شمال وخلف وقدام، بل يعنون ما بينا من وجود الجهات من حيث العبارة والمعنى دون الجهات التي يصح منها محاسة ما يلاقيها وبمعرفة تفسير الجهات تظهر جهالة المعتزلة بإنكارهم ذلك، وزعمهم أن الجهات كون للأجسام وكل جهة تغاير صاحبتها، والعرض شيء واحد فلا وجه لجعله أشياء متغايرة مع أنه في نفسه شيء واحد كما شنع الإسكافي وجعفر بن حرب وغيرهما حيث صنفوا تصانيف لإبطال جهات الفعل وكل ذلك لإنكارهم الحقيقة وجهلهم بحقائق مذاهب الخصوم، هذا تقرير مذهبهم، وإن كنا لا نحتاج إلى القول بجهات الفعل على ما قررنا.
Then the Najjāriyya, on this basis, claim that the action has aspects. For in so far as it is an act of Allah the Exalted, it is not ugly, and in so far as it is an act of the servant, it is ugly. And it is possible that an action has aspects such that it is ugly and is sin in some aspects, and is not so in other aspects. Do you not see that kufr (disbelief) is a thing, an accident, a belief, and it is Allah's proof against the disbeliever for punishment, and it is not ugly. So in so far as it is a thing — for if it were ugly in so far as it is a thing, then everything would be ugly, and then faith would be ugly and a cause of punishment, and likewise every act of obedience. And likewise, it is not ugly in so far as it is an accident, nor in so far as it is a belief, because that would entail that faith is ugly and forbidden. And likewise, kufr is a lie, and it is evidence of the disbeliever's foolishness; its indication of his foolishness is true, so the lie becomes truth in its indication of the liar's foolishness, and it is not ugly nor foolish because it deserves punishment in so far as it is a lie, not in so far as it is truth. And likewise, blackness is a thing, an accident, and a colour, and it is not black because it is a thing, nor because it is an accident, nor because it is a colour, for we find things, accidents, and colours that are not black. And by these 'aspects' they do not mean the aspects of bodies, such as above, below, right, left, behind, and front; rather, they mean what we have explained of the existence of aspects in terms of expression and meaning, not the aspects by which contact can be sensed. And by knowing the interpretation of 'aspects' the ignorance of the Muʿtazila appears in their denial of that, and their claim that aspects are a being for bodies, and each aspect is different from its companion, and an accident is one thing, so there is no way to make it multiple things despite being in itself one thing, as al-Iskāfī and Jaʿfar ibn Ḥarb and others railed when they authored works to refute the aspects of actions — all of that due to their denial of reality and their ignorance of the realities of the doctrines of opponents. This is the exposition of their doctrine, even though we have no need for the statement of aspects of actions, as we have established.
Source pages p. 934

Commentary
This passage expounds the Najjāriyya doctrine of jihāt al-fiʿl (aspects of actions) and uses it to defend the objective distinction between good and evil. The Najjāriyya hold that a single action can be evaluated differently depending on the aspect from which it is considered. As an act of Allah, it cannot be ugly; as an act of the servant, it can be. They illustrate this with kufr: it is a thing, an accident, a belief, and a divine proof (against the disbeliever), and it is not ugly from those aspects. But from the aspect of being an act of the servant, it is ugly. They also analyze kufr as a lie that indicates the disbeliever's foolishness—this indication is true, so the lie is true in one respect. The author then clarifies that these aspects are not spatial but conceptual. He criticizes the Muʿtazila for denying aspects and for misunderstanding the doctrine, citing al-Iskāfī and Jaʿfar ibn Ḥarb. Finally, he reiterates that the Māturīdī school does not need this theory.

Key Terms
جهات
نجارية
كفر
عرض
اعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Najjāriyya believe that actions are inherently good or evil in themselves without regard to aspects.
Correction: They hold that ugliness or beauty depends on the aspect from which the action is considered; the same action can be good in one aspect and bad in another.

Checkpoint
According to the Najjāriyya, why is kufr not ugly from the aspect of being a thing?
A) Because kufr is not really a thing.
B) Because if it were, then everything that is a thing (including faith) would be ugly.
C) Because only actions are ugly, not things.
D) Because things are always good.

Key Takeaway
The Najjāriyya use the concept of aspects to argue that an action's moral evaluation depends on the perspective from which it is considered, not its intrinsic nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you deeply regret. From what aspect might God still see it as not ugly? How does that affect your view of His mercy?
?
If moral value depends on aspects, how does that change the way you judge others? Can you think of a specific person whose actions you now see differently?
?
The Najjāriyya argue that kufr is not ugly in itself, but only as a servant's act. Does that make you less or more aware of your own accountability? Why?

6 min
Kufr as Lie and Truth, and the Example of Blackness
Continuation of kufr example and analogy with blackness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Najjāriyya analyze kufr as a lie that is also truth in one aspect, and how they use the example of blackness to illustrate the same principle.

Core Idea
A single thing can be both a lie and truth from different aspects.

The Najjāriyya extend their analysis of kufr by considering it as a lie (kadhib) that indicates the disbeliever's foolishness. They argue that this indication is true, so the lie becomes truth in that respect. They then give the example of blackness to show that being a thing, accident, or colour does not make something black, just as being a thing, accident, or belief does not make kufr ugly.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the kufr example
Read the passage where kufr is considered as a lie and as evidence of foolishness. Note how the author says the lie becomes truth in its indication.
Focus on the phrase 'فكان الكذب صدقا في الدلالة'.
2
Understand the logic of the lie/truth duality
The author argues that kufr is a lie from one aspect but true from another. This parallels the earlier analysis: ugliness belongs to the aspect of being a lie, not truth.
This shows that one action can have opposite evaluations depending on the aspect.
3
Read the blackness example
The passage uses blackness as a simpler analogy: blackness is a thing, accident, and colour, but not black because of those categories. Similarly, kufr is not ugly because it is a thing, etc.
This reinforces the idea that generic categories do not determine specific properties.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is kufr not ugly from the aspect of being true (sidq)?
A) Because truth can never be ugly.
B) Because the lie becomes truth in its indication of foolishness, and ugliness attaches to the lie aspect.
C) Because kufr is not actually a lie.
D) Because truth is always good.

Key Takeaway
The Najjāriyya illustrate that actions and qualities can have multiple aspects, and their moral evaluation depends on which aspect is considered, as shown by the lie/truth duality and the blackness analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you judged someone's action as entirely bad. Based on this lesson, can you identify a positive aspect of that action that would change your judgment? How does the principle of aspect-dependence challenge you to be more nuanced in your evaluations?
?
The Najjāriyya argue that kufr, though a lie, is true evidence of the disbeliever's foolishness. If your own wrong actions reveal something true about you (e.g., your weakness), are you willing to accept that truth humbly, or do you hide it? Commit to acknowledging one such truth about yourself today.
?
This lesson separates the ugliness of an act from its other aspects. Is there a habit you consider 'ugly' that might have a truthful aspect? How can you use that truth to grow closer to Allah, rather than just feeling shame?

Conceptual Aspects vs. Spatial Aspects and Refutation of Muʿtazila
Distinction of aspects and refutation of Muʿtazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Najjāriyya distinction between conceptual and spatial aspects, and why the Muʿtazila's rejection of this doctrine is based on ignorance.

Core Idea
Aspects are conceptual, not physical—denying them shows ignorance of reality.

The author clarifies that the 'aspects' discussed are not physical directions like up and down, but conceptual dimensions considered in expression and meaning. He then criticizes the Muʿtazila for misunderstanding this and attacking the doctrine, citing al-Iskāfī and Jaʿfar ibn Ḥarb. Finally, he notes that the Māturīdī school does not need this theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarification and refutation
Read the passage where the author distinguishes conceptual aspects from spatial ones, and then criticizes the Muʿtazila for their denial.
Look for the phrase 'لا يعنون بهذه الجهات كما هو جهات الأجسام'.
2
Understand the Muʿtazila objection
The Muʿtazila claimed that aspects (jihāt) are a kind of location for bodies, and an accident cannot have multiple locations. The author rejects this as a misunderstanding.
Al-Iskāfī and Jaʿfar ibn Ḥarb wrote works against this doctrine.
3
Note the Māturīdī position
The author concludes by stating that the Māturīdīs have no need for this theory, indicating it is not part of their own doctrine.
This suggests the exposition is for refutation or clarification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 934

Commentary
This passage expounds the Najjāriyya doctrine of jihāt al-fiʿl (aspects of actions) and uses it to defend the objective distinction between good and evil. The Najjāriyya hold that a single action can be evaluated differently depending on the aspect from which it is considered. As an act of Allah, it cannot be ugly; as an act of the servant, it can be. They illustrate this with kufr: it is a thing, an accident, a belief, and a divine proof (against the disbeliever), and it is not ugly from those aspects. But from the aspect of being an act of the servant, it is ugly. They also analyze kufr as a lie that indicates the disbeliever's foolishness—this indication is true, so the lie is true in one respect. The author then clarifies that these aspects are not spatial but conceptual. He criticizes the Muʿtazila for denying aspects and for misunderstanding the doctrine, citing al-Iskāfī and Jaʿfar ibn Ḥarb. Finally, he reiterates that the Māturīdī school does not need this theory.

Key Terms
جهات
معتزلة
إسكافي
جعد بن حرب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Muʿtazila thought that 'aspects' meant physical directions like positions in space.
Correction: The Najjāriyya explicitly state that aspects are conceptual, not spatial.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the Muʿtazila reject the Najjāriyya doctrine of aspects?
A) Because they believe aspects are spatial and an accident cannot have multiple locations.
B) Because they deny that kufr can be ugly.
C) Because they follow al-Iskāfī blindly.
D) Because they think actions have no aspects.

Key Takeaway
The Najjāriyya doctrine of aspects is conceptual, not physical, and the Muʿtazila's rejection stems from misunderstanding; the Māturīdī school does not rely on this theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological view that immediately strikes you as obviously wrong, do you pause to ask whether you have understood their terms as they intend them? Or do you attack a strawman without realizing it?
?
The Muʿtazila confidently argued against something they misunderstood. In your own discussions about religion, have you ever confidently argued against a position you later realized you had misrepresented? What will you do differently next time?
?
The author shows that a group held a nuanced position that was twisted by opponents. Is there a belief you hold that others commonly misunderstand? Have you contributed to that misunderstanding by not explaining it clearly? What can you do to communicate more faithfully?

Objection and Answer on Relational Descriptions
Nasafi presents an objection linking good/evil to truth/falsehood and gives a detailed answer based on essence vs. relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi defends the coherence of relational descriptions (like good/evil) without contradiction by distinguishing properties based on essence from those based on relation.

Core Idea
Descriptions tied to essence are fixed; those tied to relation can change without absurdity.

This passage tackles a key objection to the Maturidi doctrine of directions of action: if the same act can be evil from one agent and good from another, then it could also be truth and falsehood simultaneously. Nasafi replies by turning the objection on its head, then provides a foundational principle that saves the doctrine from absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objector's claim: if goodness/evil can differ by agent, why not truth/falsehood? Note the form of the analogy.
The objection uses a reductio ad absurdum: if relational differences are allowed, then truth and falsehood could also be relational, which is absurd.
2
Follow the parity response
Nasafi answers by showing that the objector already accepts relational differences in obedience/disobedience, progress/regress, etc. If that doesn't imply absurdity, then neither does the doctrine of directions.
This is a rebuttal by parity of reasoning—showing the objection proves too much.
3
Grasp the essence vs. relation principle
Nasafi then states the core principle: descriptions based on essence do not change with relations, but descriptions based on relations do change because their cause changes.
This is the key conceptual move: differentiating fixed (essence-based) from variable (relation-based) attributes.
4
Apply the analogy to kinship terms
He illustrates with father/son, uncle/nephew—same person, different relational descriptions. This shows relational differences are common and non‑contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا على من قال من القائلين بجهات الفعل لو جاز أن يكون شيء واحد شرًا من فاعل خيرًا من فاعل، جورًا من فاعل عدلاً من فاعل، لجاز أن يكون شيء صدقًا كذبًا. قيل: لو لزمنا ذلك بإثباتنا ما أثبتنا لزمكم أيضا أن تجعلوا شيئًا واحدًا صدقًا من فاعل كذبًا من فاعل قياسًا على إثباتكم واحدا طاعة الله تعالى معصية لغيره؛ لأن الطاعة له معصية للشيطان، وكذا قياسًا على إثباتكم شيئًا واحدًا تقدمًا إلى مكان تأخرًا عن غيره، وقياسًا على إثباتكم شيئًا هو فعل لشيء ترك لغيره، فإن لم يلزمكم ذلك لم يلزمنا، وحقيقة هذا الكلام أن ما كان يستحقه الشيء من الوصف لذاته لا يختلف باختلاف الإضافة لأن علة استحقاقه ذاته، وذاته لا تختلف باختلاف الإضافة فلا يختلف ما استحقه لذاته لأن علته لم تختلف فلا يختلف المعلول، وما كان استحقاقه باختلاف ما يضايقه يختلف باختلاف المتضايف، لأن علته تختلف فيختلف المعلول، وما أثبتنا اختلاف الجهات فيه كان من الأوصاف الثابتة باعتبار الإضافة كالطاعة والمعصية والحكمة والسفه والشر والخير وأشباه ذلك، فكان ذلك نظير الأب والابن والعم والخال وأشباه ذلك، والله الموفق.
If they say to one who affirms directions of action: If it is permissible that one thing be evil from one doer and good from another, injustice from one doer and justice from another, then it would be permissible that one thing be truth and falsehood. It is said: If that were necessary for us by our affirmation of what we have affirmed, it would also be necessary for you to make one thing truth from one doer and falsehood from another by analogy to your affirmation that one thing is obedience to God and disobedience to another, because obedience to Him is disobedience to Satan, and likewise by analogy to your affirmation that one thing is progress to a place and regress from another, and by analogy to your affirmation that one thing is an act for something and a leaving for another. If that is not necessary for you, then it is not necessary for us. And the reality of this speech is that what a thing deserves of description due to its essence does not differ with difference of relation, because the cause of its deserving is its essence, and its essence does not differ with difference of relation, so what it deserves due to its essence does not differ, because its cause did not differ, so the effect does not differ. And what is deserving due to difference of what is related to it differs with difference of the correlatives, because its cause differs, so the effect differs. And what we have affirmed of difference of directions in it was among the descriptions established by way of relation, like obedience and disobedience, wisdom and foolishness, evil and good, and the like of that. So that is analogous to father and son, uncle and maternal uncle, and the like of that. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 935

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the doctrine of directions of action: if the same act can be good from one agent and evil from another, then by analogy it could be truth and falsehood simultaneously, which is absurd. Nasafi then provides the answer. First, he turns the objection back on the objector by noting that they already accept that one act is obedience to God and disobedience to Satan, and other relational descriptions like progress/regress. If that does not force absurdity for them, then the same applies to the proponents of directions of action. He then gives the fundamental principle: descriptions based on essence (ذات) do not change with relations because the cause (the essence) is constant. Descriptions based on relation (إضافة) do change because the cause (the relation) changes. The differences in directions that proponents affirm are of the relational type, like obedience/disobedience, good/evil, etc. This is analogous to kinship terms like father/son, which are relational.

Key Terms
جهات الفعل
ذات
إضافة
طاعة
معصية
متضايف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Nasafi's argument is circular or merely shifts the burden without giving a real answer.
Correction: Nasafi actually provides a substantive philosophical principle (essence vs. relation) that explains why relational descriptions can differ without absurdity.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's answer, what type of description does not change with relation?
A) Descriptions based on relation
B) Descriptions based on essence
C) Descriptions based on the agent's intention
D) Descriptions based on the act itself

Key Takeaway
Relational descriptions (like good/evil from different agents) do not entail contradiction because their cause—the relation—changes, unlike essence-based descriptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate your own actions, do you sometimes treat them as if they have a fixed moral label ('good' or 'bad') regardless of your intention and relationship with Allah? Nasafi says good and evil are relational—tied to the agent. If that is true, then how does it change the way you judge yourself? In the next 24 hours, can you catch yourself labeling an action as 'good' merely because it appears to be, without considering why you are doing it? Commit now to one action you will re-evaluate through this lens.
?
If obedience to God is at the same time disobedience to Satan (as the passage states), then your every action is a battleground between two relations. What does that mean for a small, seemingly neutral act like eating or studying? Are you relating it to Allah or to your nafs? Ask yourself: 'In this moment, am I performing this act as obedience to Allah or disobedience to Him?' How can you actively turn even routine actions into acts of obedience starting today? Write down one specific routine you will transform this week.
?
Nasafi teaches that descriptions due to essence never change, but those due to relation can. Your identity as a believer—is it essential (fixed and guaranteed) or relational (dependent on your connection with Allah)? If it is relational, then it can weaken or strengthen. Does that thought unsettle you? It should, because it places a constant responsibility on you to maintain that relationship. What is one relationship-damaging habit you know you need to abandon? Pray now for the strength to leave it behind, and tell one person you trust about your commitment.
