The Mu'tazili Contradiction: Tamkīn and Luṭf
Al-Nasafi exposes the Mu'tazili contradiction between tamkīn and luṭf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the internal contradiction between the Mu'tazili concepts of tamkīn (empowerment) and luṭf (grace) as exposed by al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
If tamkīn suffices for action, luṭf is redundant; if luṭf is needed, tamkīn is insufficient.

Al-Nasafi points out a key inconsistency in the Mu'tazili theory of human action. They maintain that a morally responsible person must have tamkīn (empowerment) to act, yet they also posit an additional divine grace (luṭf) that is necessary for making a choice. This passage shows how these two claims conflict, undermining their own framework of human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage
Read the original Arabic text once to get the flow of al-Nasafi's argument. Then read the literal translation to ensure understanding.
Focus on the sequence: tamkīn claim → luṭf definition.
2
Identify the Two Mu'tazili Claims
The passage states that the Mu'tazila require tamkīn for action, and then they add luṭf as an additional factor. Identify how they define luṭf.
Notice the phrase 'thumma warā'a l-tamkīn wa-l-qudra maʿānī' — beyond empowerment and capability are meanings.
3
Understand the Contradiction
Ask yourself: if tamkīn alone suffices to reach the act, why is luṭf needed? And if luṭf is needed, then tamkīn cannot be sufficient. This is the contradiction al-Nasafi exposes.
The contradiction is egregious (fāḥish) because it violates logical coherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لهم تناقض فاحش فإنهم زعموا أن المكلف لا بد له من التمكن من الفعل وبثبوت التمكن يتوصل إلى الفعل، ثم وراء التمكن والقدرة معاني ويسمونها لطفا، ويفسرون اللطف أنه ما يختار عنده المكلف ما كلف من أخذ وترك لولاه لكان لا يختار أو يكون أقرب إلى هذا الاختيار فما هذا حاله يسمونه لطفا
Then they have an egregious contradiction: they claimed that the morally responsible person (al-mukallaf) necessarily requires empowerment (al-tamkīn) to act, and by the establishment of empowerment he reaches the act. Then, beyond empowerment and capability (al-qudra), there are meanings which they call grace (luṭf), and they interpret grace as that by which the morally responsible person chooses what he is commanded—of taking and leaving—without which he would not choose, or it brings him closer to this choice. So that which is in this state they call grace.
Source pages p. 1002

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of a contradiction in the Mu'tazili doctrine concerning human action. Al-Nasafi states that the Mu'tazila hold that the morally responsible person (al-mukallaf) must first have empowerment (tamkīn) to perform an act, and through empowerment he can perform the act. However, they also affirm that beyond empowerment and capability (qudra) there are additional meanings which they call luṭf (divine grace). They define luṭf as something that causes the servant to choose what he is commanded (whether to do or refrain), such that without it he would not choose, or it brings him closer to that choice. Al-Nasafi points out the contradiction: if empowerment alone suffices for action, then luṭf is redundant; if luṭf is needed, then empowerment is not sufficient, which undermines their doctrine of human free will and responsibility.

Key Terms
التمكن
القدرة
اللطف
الاختيار
المكلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazili concept of luṭf is similar to predestination or divine coercion.
Correction: The passage shows luṭf is meant to aid choice, but by making it necessary, it contradicts their emphasis on human empowerment (tamkīn), not that it forces action.

Checkpoint
According to the text, how do the Mu'tazila define luṭf (grace)?
A) It is the power to act.
B) It is what helps the servant to choose what he is commanded, without which he would not choose or brings him closer to choice.
C) It is God's eternal will.
D) It is the opposite of tamkīn.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili doctrines of tamkīn and luṭf are internally contradictory because they cannot both be necessary for human action without undermining each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your own ability (tamkīn) is sufficient for every good deed, or do you humbly acknowledge that without Allah's grace (luṭf) you would not choose what is right? What would change if you truly recognized your need for His help in every moral choice?
?
When you fall short in obedience, do you rely on your own resolve to improve, or do you turn to Allah for the luṭf that alone makes you choose rightly? How does the Mu'tazili contradiction exposed in this passage challenge any self-sufficiency in your heart?
?
Is there an area of your life where you claim full responsibility for your actions, yet secretly hope for divine grace when you struggle? This passage shows that such a position is contradictory. Will you today admit your dependence on Allah's grace in all your choices and ask Him for it?

Tamkin and Lutf in Mu'tazilite Theology
Explains Mu'tazilite concepts of tamkin and lutf, with views of Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite concepts of tamkin (enabling) and lutf (grace), and the debate between Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i regarding the source and obligation of lutf.

Core Idea
Tamkin is minimal ability; lutf is extra assistance that tips the scales toward obedience.

The Mu'tazilites developed a sophisticated theory of divine obligation that required two elements: tamkin (enabling) and lutf (grace). The first is the basic capacity to act, while the second is additional circumstantial help that inclines the servant to choose obedience. This passage from the Tabṣirah outlines these concepts and records a key difference between Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i on whether lutf must come only from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the technical terms tamkin and lutf.
2
Identify the definitions
Locate how the passage defines tamkin and lutf. What is the difference between them?
Tamkin is necessary; lutf is not necessary but facilitates.
3
Analyze the examples
Examine the examples given for lutf: illnesses, wealth, admonitions. How do they influence choice?
These states affect motives and thus count as lutfs.
4
Compare the two scholars' views
Contrast Abu Hashim's and al-Jubba'i's positions on the source of lutf and whether God is obligated to provide it.
Abu Hashim allows lutf from others; al-Jubba'i restricts it to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويزعمون أن الله تعالى إذا كلف عبدًا لا بد له من أمرين: أحدهما ما يتمكن به والآخر ما عنده يختار أو يقوى اختيار فالأول يسمى تمكينًا، وهو الذي لا بد منه في فعل ما كلف. والثاني: يسمى لطفا لأن الفعل قد يصح على الوجه الذي كلف دونه لكنه إذا كان حصل يصير حاله في دواعيه بخلاف حاله إذا لم يكن، وذلك كالأمراض والمصائب والغموم والفقر والغنى وغير ذلك من الأسباب، فإن الإنسان قد يختار عند حالة من هذه الأحوال ما لا يختار عند غيرها فكانت هذه الأحوال المتفرقة في لطف كل واحد منهم ما اختص به من الحالة. ثم عند أبي هاشم قد يكون اللطف من الله تعالى وقد يجوز أن يكون من غير الله تعالى، فإنه إذا كان في معلوم الله تعالى أن رزق إنسانًا إذا اتسع عليه كان أقرب إلى طاعته يوسع عليه الرزق ثم قد يكون ذلك بالهبة والوصية، وهما فعل غير الله تعالى ولا تفاوت في حق اللطف بين هذا وبين ما يوصل إليه الله تعالى، وكذا قد يحصل ذلك بالتنبيه والوعظ والتذكير من الصالحين. وقال الجبائي: لا يكون اللطف من قبل غير الله تعالى في تكليف المكلف إذ لو كان لما وجب على الكل الفزع في الألطاف إليه تعالى، ثم عند أبي هاشم ما كان من الألطاف من فعل الله تعالى يجب على الله تعالى تحصيله وما كان من فعل غير الله فليس بواجب تحصيله لكن التكليف به كان معلقا فإن كان في المعلوم حصوله كلف الله تعالى وإلا لم يكلف هذا هو تقرير مذاهبهم في اللطف.
They claim that when God Most High obligates a servant, he must have two things: one by which he is enabled (tamkin), and the other by which he chooses or his choice is strengthened. The first is called tamkin, which is necessary for performing what he is obligated to do. The second is called lutf (grace), because the act may be valid in the manner he is obligated without it, but when it occurs, his state regarding his motives becomes different from what it would be if it did not occur. Such states are like illnesses, calamities, sorrows, poverty, wealth, and other causes. For a man may choose in one of these states what he would not choose in another, so these varying states each constitute a lutf for the individual in accordance with his specific state. Then according to Abu Hashim, lutf may come from God Most High or may possibly come from other than God. For if it is in God's knowledge that providing for a person abundantly would make him closer to obedience, He expands his provision. This may occur through a gift or bequest, both actions of other than God, and there is no difference in respect of lutf between this and what God conveys to him. Similarly, this may occur through the admonition, preaching, and reminder of righteous people. Al-Jubba'i said: Lutf does not come from other than God in obligating the obligated, because if it did, everyone would be required to resort to God for the lutfs. Then according to Abu Hashim, that which is from God's actions of lutfs must be procured by God Most High, while that from other than God is not obligatory to procure, but the obligation regarding it is suspended: if it is known that it will occur, God obligates; otherwise He does not obligate. This is the exposition of their schools concerning lutf.
Source pages p. 1004

Commentary
This passage explains the Mu'tazilite doctrine of tamkin and lutf. The author first states that according to the Mu'tazilites, when God obligates a servant, two things are necessary: tamkin (enabling) and lutf (grace). Tamkin is defined as the minimal ability required for performing the obligated act. Lutf is defined as additional circumstances that make the act easier and incline the servant towards choice. Examples include health, wealth, poverty, or admonitions that affect one's motives. The author then reports a difference between Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i. Abu Hashim holds that lutf can come from God or from other beings (e.g., gifts from people), and that when it is from God, God is obligated to provide it. Al-Jubba'i restricts lutf to God alone, arguing that otherwise all would be obliged to seek lutfs from God. Abu Hashim distinguishes obligatory lutfs (from God) from non-obligatory ones (from others), and says obligation is conditional on God's knowledge of its occurrence. The passage concludes by stating this is a summary of their views.

Key Terms
تمكين
لطف
أبو هاشم
الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that tamkin and lutf are the same thing or that lutf is necessary for an act to be valid.
Correction: Tamkin is the minimal ability required for any obligated act; lutf is an additional facilitator that makes obedience easier but is not strictly necessary for the act's validity.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites, what is the primary difference between tamkin and lutf?
A) Tamkin is from God, lutf is from humans.
B) Tamkin is necessary for obligation, lutf is not necessary but facilitates choice.
C) Tamkin is for actions, lutf is for beliefs.
D) Tamkin and lutf are identical.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites distinguish between tamkin (enabling ability) and lutf (grace), with disagreement among their theologians about the source and obligatoriness of lutf.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when a sudden change—illness, wealth, or a word from a friend—completely shifted your choices toward obedience or away from it. The Mu'tazilites call that circumstance 'lutf.' Do you see God's hand in that, or do you credit only the human cause? Examine your heart: are you quick to attribute your obedience to your own strength and your disobedience to your circumstances?
?
Abu Hashim teaches that lutf can come through other people—a gift, a bequest, or a reminder. Who in your life has been such a lutf for you? Have you ever thanked either God or that person? Now ask yourself: are you being a lutf for others? Whom might God be calling you to admonish or help today, not to judge them, but to tip their hearts toward Him?
?
You believe you have tamkin—the basic ability to do what God asks. But do you actually live as if you do? Or do you hide behind excuses like 'I'm weak' or 'I can't'? What if the real lack is not ability but the will to choose obedience? What would it cost you to stop excusing yourself and to ask God for the lutf to make the hard choice—starting today?

The Internal Contradiction in Mu'tazilite Lutf Doctrine
Critiques the Mu'tazilite view on lutf, showing the reward paradox.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim that lutf is necessary for action contradicts their own assumption about reward and tamkin.

Core Idea
If a harder act brings more reward, withholding lutf is more beneficial.

The Mu'tazilites argued that God must provide lutf (grace) to enable obedience. The author counters that acts can be performed without lutf, and doing so yields greater reward. This raises a dilemma: if God withholds lutf, the servant gains a higher reward; if He grants it, the reward is lower. The Mu'tazilite position thus leads to an absurd conclusion about divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
Recall that Mu'tazilites believe God must provide lutf to enable acts of obedience, otherwise He would be unjust.
2
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: first the contradiction, then the argument about tamkin and reward.
3
Analyze the reward logic
The author says performing an act without lutf is harder and more rewarding. So why would God grant lutf and reduce reward? This exposes inconsistency in Mu'tazilite reasoning.
The phrase 'أعلى المنزلتين' refers to the higher station of reward.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Even if God knows the servant won't act without lutf, withholding it still exposes him to higher reward, while granting it lowers him. Thus the Mu'tazilite position is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هذا منهم مناقضة عظيمة حيث أرجعوا اللطف على الله تعالى، وذلك أن الفعل يتصور حصوله بالتمكن بدون هذا اللطف على ما حكينا، ولا شك أن تحصيل الفعل بدون اللطف أشق على البدن وأكثر ثوابًا، وتحصيله مع اللطف أخف وأيسر على البدن فيكون أقل ثوابًا فكان الأصلح للعبد والتعويض لأعلى المنزلتين أن يمنعه اللطف ليكون ثواب فعله أجزل والمنفعة به أوفر وأكمل، وإن كان يعلم الله تعالى أنه لا يفعل بدون اللطف ولكن مع هذا منعه تعريض لأعلى المنزلتين وإعطائه إزالة له على أعلى المنزلتين إلى أدونهما، وهذا ليس يصلح كما أنه تعالى يمهل من يعلم أنه لو بلغ لكفر ومن يعلم أنه بعد ما زجى عمره في الإسلام ثمانين سنة يرتد بعد ذلك إذ هو بهذا الإمهال عرضة لأعلى المنزلتين، وإن كان يعلم أنه يكفر ويرتد فإن كانت العبرة لما يحصل من العاقبة المعلومة، وكان إعطاء اللطف أصلح إذ لو لم يعطه لما فعل لكان ينبغي أن لا يمهل هناك فإنه إن كان لا يمهل لا يكفر، وسقط التعريض على مصلحة يعلم أنها لا تحصل في الفصلين جميعًا، وإن كانت العبرة لنفس المصلحة وكان التعريض لها هو الأصلح، ولا عبرة للعلم بحصولها وعدم حصولها، كان منع اللطف هاهنا والتعريض لأعلى المنزلتين أصلح فأما اعتبار العلم في باب إعطاء اللطف وإسقاط اعتباره في باب الإمهال إلى أن يكفر فبناء للأمر على تشهي النفس وميلان الهوى دون الدليل والعقل عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك.
Then this is a great contradiction from them, since they attributed lutf to God Most High. That is because the act is conceivable without this lutf, by virtue of tamkin alone, as we have related. There is no doubt that performing the act without lutf is harder on the body and brings greater reward, while performing it with lutf is lighter and easier on the body, thus less rewarding. Therefore, the more beneficial for the servant and the compensation for the higher of the two stations would be to withhold lutf from him, so that the reward for his act be greater and the benefit more abundant and complete. Even if God Most High knows that he will not act without lutf, nevertheless withholding it exposes him to the higher station, while granting it lowers him from the higher station to the lower. This is not proper. For He also grants respite to one whom He knows would disbelieve if he reached a certain point, and to one whom He knows that after spending eighty years in Islam he would afterwards apostatize, for by this respite he is exposed to the higher station. Even if He knows that he will disbelieve and apostatize, then if consideration is given to the outcome that occurs, and granting lutf is more beneficial because if it were not granted he would not act, then He should not grant respite there, for if He does not grant respite, he would not disbelieve, and the exposure to a benefit that is known not to occur would be lost in both cases. If consideration is given to the benefit itself and exposure to it is the more beneficial, and knowledge of its occurrence or non-occurrence is irrelevant, then withholding lutf here and exposing to the higher station is more beneficial. As for considering knowledge in the matter of granting lutf while disregarding it in the matter of granting respite until apostasy, that is built on whim and caprice, not on evidence and reason. May God protect us from that.
Source pages p. 1004

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (naqd) of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of lutf. The author argues that the Mu'tazilite position is self-contradictory. First, he states that acts can be performed with tamkin alone, without lutf. So lutf is not strictly necessary. If an act is done without lutf, it is harder and brings more reward; with lutf, it is easier and less rewarding. Therefore, for the servant's ultimate benefit, it would be better for God to withhold lutf so the servant can attain the higher reward. Even if God knows the servant will not act without lutf, withholding it still exposes him to the possibility of higher reward, while granting it guarantees only a lower reward. The author then draws an analogy with God granting respite to a person He knows will eventually disbelieve: in that case, the Mu'tazilites do not deny respite, yet they apply a different standard to lutf. The author analyzes two possible principles: either consideration is given to the known outcome, or to the benefit of exposure itself. If the first, then God should not grant respite to the eventual disbeliever (since He knows the bad outcome), but He does. If the second, then withholding lutf is more beneficial because it exposes to higher reward. The Mu'tazilites are inconsistent: they apply knowledge to lutf but not to respite. This shows their position is based on desire, not reason. The author concludes by seeking God's protection from such error.

Key Terms
مناقضة
تمكن
ثواب
تعريض
أعلى المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God knows a person will not obey without lutf, then granting lutf is necessary for justice.
Correction: The author shows that even then, withholding lutf exposes the person to higher reward, which is better for them, so God's wisdom would favor withholding.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is performing an act without lutf more rewarding?
A) Because it is harder on the body
B) Because it shows more faith
C) Because God loves difficulty
D) Because it is done with tamkin only

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite claim that God must grant lutf leads to a contradiction because withholding lutf would result in a higher reward for the servant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself seeking ease in worship, do you ever consider that you might be choosing a lesser reward? Will you embrace difficulty in obedience today for the sake of a greater thawab?
?
If God withholds ease from you in a certain act of worship, do you complain or trust that He is exposing you to a higher station? What specific trial can you reframe as divine ta'rid to a greater reward?
?
Are you willing to risk disobedience (by not having lutf) for the chance of a higher rank, or do you prefer the guaranteed but lower reward of ease? Which choice dominates your heart right now, and what will you do about it?

The Inconsistency in Mu'tazilite Use of Divine Knowledge
Critiques Mu'tazilite inconsistency between lutf and imhal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author exposes the Mu'tazilites' inconsistent application of God's foreknowledge in their doctrines of lutf and imhal (respite).

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites apply divine knowledge to lutf but ignore it for respite, showing whim, not reason.

The author extends his refutation by drawing an analogy with God's granting of respite (imhal) to people He knows will eventually disbelieve. The Mu'tazilites do not object to this, yet they apply a different standard to lutf. This reveals an inconsistency: they consider divine knowledge when it supports their view, but ignore it otherwise. The author concludes that their position is based on desire, not evidence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the respite analogy
God grants respite to people He knows will disbelieve later. This exposes them to higher reward if they reform, but God knows they won't.
The analogy parallels the lutf case where God knows the servant won't act without lutf.
2
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. The author considers two principles: considering the outcome vs. considering the benefit of exposure itself.
3
Analyze the two possibilities
If outcome matters, then God should not grant respite to the eventual disbeliever (since He knows the bad outcome). But He does. If exposure itself matters, then withholding lutf is better. Either way, Mu'tazilites are inconsistent.
4
Identify the inconsistency
Mu'tazilites apply God's knowledge when granting lutf but not when granting respite. This shows their doctrine is built on whim, not consistent reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Argument on Divine Grace (Lutf)
Refutes the Mu'tazilite position that God must grant lutf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi school argues that withholding divine grace (lutf) can be more beneficial for the servant's reward than granting it.

Core Idea
Withholding grace can be more merciful than granting it.

The Mu'tazilites claimed that God is obliged to grant grace (lutf) to enable obedience. Imam al-Nasafi refutes this by showing that performing acts without grace is harder but yields greater reward, so withholding grace is actually more beneficial. In this passage, he exposes the internal contradiction in the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Note the key term 'لطف' (lutf) and its context.
2
Understand the core argument
The author argues that an act can be performed without grace; doing so is harder but earns greater reward. Thus, withholding grace is more beneficial for the servant.
Consider the logic: harder act → more reward → better for the servant.
3
Analyze the analogy with respite
The author compares withholding grace to giving respite to a potential disbeliever. Both expose the servant to the highest station, even if the outcome is known.
Why does this analogy challenge the Mu'tazilite position?

هذا منهم مناقضة عظيمة حيث أرجعوا اللطف على الله تعالى، وذلك أن الفعل يتصور حصوله بالتمكن بدون هذا اللطف على ما حكينا، ولا شك أن تحصيل الفعل بدون اللطف أشق على البدن وأكثر ثوابًا، وتحصيله مع اللطف أخف وأيسر على البدن فيكون أقل ثوابًا فكان الأصلح للعبد [؟] والتعويض لأعلى المنزلتين أن يمنعه اللطف ليكون ثواب فعله أجزل والمنفعة به أوفر وأكمل، وإن كان يعلم الله تعالى أنه لا يفعل بدون اللطف ولكن مع هذا منعه تعريض لأعلى المنزلتين وإعطائه إزالة له على أعلى المنزلتين إلى أدونهما، وهذا ليس يصلح كما أنه تعالى يمهل من يعلم أنه لو بلغ لكفر ومن يعلم أنه بعد ما زجى عمره في الإسلام ثمانين سنة يرتد بعد ذلك إذ هو بهذا الإمهال عرضة لأعلى المنزلتين، وإن كان يعلم أنه يكفر ويرتد فإن كانت العبرة لما يحصل من العاقبة المعلومة، وكان إعطاء اللطف أصلح إذ لو لم يعطه لما فعل لكان ينبغي أن لا يمهل هناك فإنه إن كان لا يمهل لا يكفر، وسقط التعريض على مصلحة يعلم أنها لا تحصل في الفصلين جميعًا، وإن كانت العبرة لنفس المصلحة وكان التعريض لها هو الأصلح، ولا عبرة للعلم بحصولها وعدم حصولها، كان منع اللطف هاهنا والتعريض لأعلى المنزلتين أصلح فأما اعتبار العلم في باب إعطاء اللطف وإسقاط اعتباره في باب الإمهال إلى أن يكفر فبناء للأمر على تشهي النفس وميلان الهوى دون الدليل والعقل عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك.
This is a great contradiction from them where they attributed grace (al-lutf) to God Almighty. That is because the act is conceivable to occur with capability without this grace according to what we have related. There is no doubt that performing the act without grace is harder on the body and greater in reward, while performing it with grace is lighter and easier on the body, thus it is lesser in reward. So the most beneficial for the servant and the compensation for the highest of the two stations is that He withholds grace from him so that the reward of his act is more abundant and the benefit is more ample and complete. And if God Almighty knows that he will not act without grace, nevertheless withholding it from him is an exposure to the highest station, and granting it is a removal of him from the highest station to the lower one. This is not proper, just as He Almighty gives respite to one whom He knows that if he reached [a certain age] he would disbelieve, and to one whom He knows that after spending eighty years in Islam he will apostatize afterwards. For by this respite he is exposed to the highest station, even though He knows he will disbelieve and apostatize. If the consideration is for what results from the known outcome, and granting grace is more beneficial since if He had not granted it he would not have acted, then it would follow that He should not give respite there; for if He does not give respite, he will not disbelieve, and the exposure to a benefit that is known not to occur falls away in both cases. And if the consideration is for the benefit itself and exposure to it is more beneficial, and there is no regard for knowledge of its occurrence or non-occurrence, then withholding grace here and exposure to the highest station is more beneficial. As for considering knowledge in the matter of granting grace and disregarding it in the matter of giving respite until he disbelieves, it is an ordering based on the desire of the soul and the inclination of caprice without proof and reason. May God Almighty protect us from that.
Source pages p. 1005

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God is obliged to grant grace (lutf) to enable human beings to perform acts of obedience. The author begins by stating that this position involves a great contradiction. He argues that an act can be performed without divine grace; performing it without grace is harder but earns greater reward, while performing it with grace is easier but earns lesser reward. Therefore, it is actually more beneficial for the servant and a form of compensation for the highest station (i.e., the highest rank in Paradise) for God to withhold grace, so that the servant's reward is greater. Even if God knows that the servant will not act without grace, withholding grace still exposes him to the possibility of attaining the highest station, whereas granting grace would move him to a lower station. The author draws an analogy with God's giving respite to those He knows will disbelieve or apostatize: by granting time, He exposes them to the highest station, even though He knows the outcome. If the criterion were the known outcome, then granting grace would be considered more beneficial, but that would imply God should not give respite at all—which is absurd. If the criterion is the benefit itself regardless of knowledge, then withholding grace is more beneficial. Mixing these criteria is arbitrary and based on desire, not reason.

ثواب
تعريض
منزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine grace (lutf) is always beneficial for the servant and that God is obliged to grant it.
Correction: The author shows that withholding grace can be more beneficial because it leads to greater reward through harder effort, and that God is not obliged to grant it based on the Mu'tazilite reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it more beneficial for God to withhold grace (lutf) from the servant?
A) Because the servant does not need grace to perform acts.
B) Because performing acts without grace is harder and yields greater reward.
C) Because God does not know whether the servant will obey.
D) Because the servant can never obey without grace.

Key Takeaway
Withholding divine grace can be more beneficial for the servant because it leads to greater reward through harder effort, challenging the Mu'tazilite claim that God must grant grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find an act of worship difficult and feel no divine ease, do you become frustrated or do you recognize it as an opportunity for greater reward? Will you embrace difficulty as a gift from today?
?
God gives respite even to those He knows will disbelieve. Are you using the time and ease He gives you to draw nearer to Him, or are you taking it for granted? What specific change will you make in your daily routine to respond to His respite with gratitude and obedience?
?
The Mu'tazilites tried to impose their own reasoning on God. Are there areas in your life where you insist on how God should treat you rather than submitting to His wisdom? Which aspect of your relationship with God do you need to surrender to His decree today?

Proof from Consensus: Refuting the Mu'tazilite View on Divine Grace
A refutation of Mu'tazilite lutf using universal supplication practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the universal practice of supplication refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God must grant divine grace (lutf).

Core Idea
The universal practice of supplication disproves divine obligation to grant grace.

The author presents a powerful argument from consensus (ijma'): Muslims and earlier religious communities have unanimously practiced supplication (du'a) for help in obedience and removal of harm. By examining the logical implications of this practice, he shows that the Mu'tazilite doctrine of obligatory grace leads to absurd consequences, thereby refuting it. This passage demonstrates the use of practical religious practice as a theological proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the logical structure: the author begins with the consensus, then presents a dichotomy.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the argument up to that point is complete for this lesson.
2
Identify the two main possibilities
The author considers whether God already grants what is asked or not. Trace the consequences of each: if granted, supplication is foolish; if not, either God must grant or may not. Find these branches.
The key is that if God must grant, supplication becomes a demand, which is problematic.
3
Understand the refutation
See how each branch leads to an absurdity or directly falsifies the Mu'tazilite view. The author concludes that if God may not grant, the Mu'tazilite position collapses. Also note the extension to health and illness.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل أهل الحق من الإجماع: وأما الإجماع فقد أجمع المسلمون وأهل الأديان السماوية قبلهم على الدعاء الله تعالى، وطلب المعونة على الطاعات والعصمة من المعاصي، وكشف ما بهم من الضر وإزالة ما بهم وبأهل عنايتهم من المرض وتبديل ذلك بالعافية، ثم الأمر لا يخلو إما أن كان ما سألوا من المعونة والعصمة آتاهم الله تعالى أو كان لم يؤتهم؟ فإن كان أتاهم فسؤالهم سفه بل كفران للنعمة إذ السؤال لما كان عند ذوي العقول لما لم يكن موجودًا فيسأل كان الاشتغال بالسؤال إلحاقا لهذه النعمة الموجودة بالمعدوم حيث اشتغل بسؤاله، وجل الله تعالى عن أن يأمر في كتبه المنزلة عباده المخلصين والأنبياء والمرسلين عليهم السلام أن يشتغلوا بما هو في الحقيقة سفه وكفران للنعمة، فإن كان لم يؤتهم لا يخلو إما أن يجوز له أن لا يؤتيهم أو كان لا يجوز فإن كان لا يجوز له أن لا يؤتيهم بل يجب عليه ذلك على وجه كان بمنعه ظالمًا مانعا حقا مستحقا لكان السؤال والدعاء في الحقيقة كأنهم قالوا: اللهم لا تظلمنا بمنع حقنا المستحق عليك ولا تجر علينا، ومن ظن أن أهل الدين الحق والأنبياء والمرسلين عليهم السلام استجازوا من أنفسهم أن يشتغلوا بمثل هذا الكلام مناجین به ربهم فقد كفر من ساعته، وكذا من ظن أن الله تعالى أمر عباده أن يدعوه بمثل هذا الدعاء. وإن كان يجوز له أن لا يؤتيهم ذلك فقط بطل مذهبهم وكذلك سؤال الصحة ودفع المرض وكشف الضر إن كان ما به من الحال مفسدة لهم أو لم يكن مصلحة لهم فقد ثبت بطلان قولهم، وإن كان ذلك مصلحة لهم بل أصلح لهم وما يضاده من الحال مفسدة فإذا أمر الله تعالى عباده بل رسله وأنبيائه عليهم السلام أن يسألوا دفع المصلحة وإعطاء المفسدة، وكذا الرسل والأنبياء عليهم السلام وأهل ما صح من الأديان أجمعوا على سؤال هذا، وهذا مما لا يخفى فساده أن الله تعالى لو بدل الحال بدعائهم لكان مبدلاً للمصلحة بالمفسدة وهذا عندهم سفه وهو جور وظلم، فيصير حاصل هذا الدعاء أنهم يطلبون من الله تعالى أن
The proof of the people of truth from consensus: As for consensus, Muslims and the people of the heavenly religions before them have unanimously agreed upon supplication to God Almighty, asking for help in acts of obedience and protection from sins, and the removal of harm that afflicts them, and the removal of what afflicts them and the people of their care from illness, and the exchange of that for well-being. Then the matter is not free: either what they asked for of help and protection God Almighty gave them, or He did not give them. If He gave them, then their asking is foolishness, rather ingratitude for the blessing, because asking is, in the minds of intelligent people, for what does not exist, so one asks. Occupying oneself with asking is then attaching this existing blessing to non-existence by virtue of asking for it. Far be it from God Almighty to command in His revealed books His sincere servants, the prophets and messengers (peace be upon them) to occupy themselves with what is essentially foolishness and ingratitude for the blessing. If He did not give them, then it is not free: either it is permissible for Him not to give them, or it is not permissible. If it is not permissible for Him not to give them, but rather it is obligatory upon Him to do so, such that by withholding He would be an oppressor, preventing a due right, then the asking and supplication would effectively be as if they said: 'O God, do not oppress us by withholding our rightful due from You, and do not be unjust to us.' Whoever thinks that the people of true religion, the prophets and messengers (peace be upon them) permitted themselves to occupy with such speech in intimate prayer to their Lord has disbelieved instantly. Likewise, whoever thinks that God Almighty commanded His servants to supplicate with such a supplication. And if it is permissible for Him not to give them that, then their doctrine is nullified. Similarly, the asking for health, repelling illness, and removing harm: if the state they are in is a detriment to them or not a benefit to them, then their statement is proven false. And if that state is a benefit to them, indeed the most beneficial, and its opposite is a detriment, then God Almighty commanding His servants—indeed His messengers and prophets (peace be upon them)—to ask for the removal of benefit and the granting of detriment, and likewise the messengers and prophets (peace be upon them) and the people of sound religions have unanimously agreed upon asking for this. This is clearly corrupt: that God Almighty, were He to change the state by their supplication, would be exchanging benefit for detriment, which in their view is foolishness and is injustice and oppression. So the outcome of this supplication is that they ask God Almighty to...
Source pages p. 1005

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from consensus (ijma') to refute the Mu'tazilite view on divine grace (lutf). The author begins by stating that Muslims and earlier religious communities have unanimously practiced supplication (du'a) to God for help in obedience, protection from sin, and removal of harm. He then analyzes the implications of this practice. If God already grants what is asked, then supplication is foolish (safah) and ingratitude (kufran al-ni'ma), because asking for something already present is treating an existing blessing as non-existent. If God does not grant it, two possibilities arise: either it is permissible for God not to grant it, or it is not permissible. If it is not permissible—i.e., God is obliged to grant it—then supplication becomes a demand for a rightful due, implying God would be unjust if He withheld it. This would make the prophets and righteous people demanders of rights, which is a form of disbelief to impute to them. If it is permissible for God not to grant it, then the Mu'tazilite position that God must grant grace (lutf) is false. The author extends the argument to asking for health and removal of harm: if sickness is detrimental, asking for its removal is beneficial; if it is beneficial, then asking for its removal would be asking for harm, which is corrupt. The passage concludes by noting the absurdity of the Mu'tazilite position, which would make God an exchanger of benefit for harm.

دعاء
سفه
حق

Checkpoint

A) Supplication is still beneficial.
B) The Mu'tazilite doctrine is nullified.
C) God is unjust.
D) The prophets sinned.

Key Takeaway
The practice of supplication across religions demonstrates that God is not obligated to grant any particular state, refuting the Mu'tazilite doctrine of obligatory grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a for something, do you ever feel a sense of entitlement that Allah must answer it? How does this passage challenge that feeling, and what change in attitude does it demand from you?
?
Think of a time you received something you had been praying for. Did you treat it as a gift or as a right that was owed? If as a right, what does that reveal about your view of Allah's sovereignty, and how can you now cultivate gratitude instead?
?
If Allah is not obligated to grant your requests, and supplication is not a demand but an act of humble need, what does that mean for the way you will approach Him tonight? Will you continue to ask with expectation, or will you surrender to His will with deeper trust?

The First Argument: Divine Gifts to Known Unbelievers
First wajh: gifts to known unbelievers cause corruption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against the Mu'tazilite aslah theory: that God giving good things to those He knows will remain unbelievers actually causes corruption, not benefit.

Core Idea
Gifts to a destined unbeliever make him worse, not better.

The Mu'tazilites held that God must always do what is best for His creatures. Nasafi counters with a logical dilemma: if God gives blessings to someone He knows will never believe, those blessings only increase his ingratitude and corruption. This argument shows that God sometimes does what is 'more corrupting' rather than 'most beneficial', undermining the absolute obligation of al-aslah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for the first argument
Read the first wajh carefully in Arabic and its English translation. Notice the key terms 'أفسد' and 'أصلح'.
The passage begins after the introductory phrase.
2
Understand the logical structure
The argument assumes God knows that a particular person will never believe. If God gives him good things anyway, those goods increase his sin and punishment, thus making him more corrupt. So God does not do what is most beneficial (al-aslah) but what is more corrupting.
3
Reflect on the implication for al-aslah
Ask yourself: If God must always do what is best, then giving to a known unbeliever would be unjust. But God does give to such people in reality. Thus, the Mu'tazilite theory is incompatible with observable divine actions.
This argument does not deny God's mercy; it simply shows logical inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فبعد ذلك تقرر هذا الكلام من وجهين أحدهما أن إعطاء من علم أنه لا يؤمن يكون أفسد له على ما قررنا، وقد أعطاه فإذا فعل به ما هو الأفسد لا ما هو الأصلح. والثاني: أن ذلك إذا كان على هذا التقرير إفسادًا لا إصلاحًا وليس في مقدور الله تعالى سوى هذا، فلم يكن إذا في مقدوره الأصلح بل في مقدوره الأفسد، فإيجاب المعتزلة إياه ما لا قدرة له على تحصيله مع قولهم أن ليس الله تعالى ولاية تكليف العبد ما لا قدرة له عليه جهل عظيم.
After that, this statement is established from two aspects: one of them is that giving to one whom He knows will not believe is more corrupting for him as we have stated, and He has given it to him, so He has done what is more corrupting for him, not what is most beneficial. The second: that this, according to this formulation, is corruption, not reform, and there is nothing in the power of God Almighty besides this, so there is not in His power the most beneficial, but rather in His power the more corrupting. Thus, the Mu'tazilites imposing upon Him what He has no power to accomplish, along with their statement that God Almighty does not have the authority to charge a servant with what he has no power over, is great ignorance.
Source pages p. 1009

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments (wajhayn) against the Mu'tazilite theory of al-aslah (the doctrine that God must do what is best for His creation). The first argument states: if God gives good things to someone He knows will remain in unbelief, that actually makes the person worse (more corrupt) because he persists in disbelief despite receiving blessings. Thus, God is doing what is more corrupting, not what is most beneficial. The second argument: since God's power in this case only results in corruption (because the person remains in unbelief), it follows that the 'most beneficial' is not within God's power; rather, the more corrupting is within His power. The Mu'tazilites, by insisting that God must do what is best, are imposing upon Him something beyond His power, which contradicts their own principle that God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity. Nasafi calls this 'great ignorance'.

Key Terms
أفسد
أصلح
أعطى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite view that God must always do what is best for each individual.
Correction: Nasafi shows that God's giving to known unbelievers results in corruption, not benefit, thus the Mu'tazilite obligation is false.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's first argument, when God gives to someone He knows will remain an unbeliever, what is the effect?
A) It makes the person grateful.
B) It makes the person more corrupt.
C) It makes the person neutral.
D) It obligates God to do better.

Key Takeaway
The first argument shows that God sometimes does what is more corrupting, thereby refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that He must always do what is most beneficial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a blessing, do you pause to ask yourself whether it is drawing you closer to God or feeding your pride? This passage shows that even a gift can be corrupting if it increases ingratitude. What blessing in your life right now might be turning you away from humility?
?
If you knew that a gift you give to someone would only make them more rebellious, would you still give it? How does God’s giving to known unbelievers challenge your assumptions about His purposes? Are you willing to accept that God’s wisdom may involve allowing people to sink deeper, rather than always saving them?
?
The argument refutes the idea that God must always do what is best for you. How does that land on your heart? Does it make you anxious, or does it free you from expecting a life of ease? Are you actually prepared to trust God even when He gives you what seems harmful?

The Second Argument: God's Power and the Mu'tazilite Imposition
Second wajh: only corruption is in God's power, so Mu'tazilite demand is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument: that since only corruption is within God's power in such cases, the Mu'tazilites are imposing an impossible obligation on God, contradicting their own principle.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites demand what God cannot do, showing their inconsistency.

The second argument builds on the first: if giving to a known unbeliever results only in corruption, then 'the most beneficial' (al-aslah) is not within God's power in that situation. Yet the Mu'tazilites insist that God must do al-aslah. Nasafi points out the contradiction: they are requiring God to do what He cannot, while they themselves deny that God can burden a soul beyond its capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the second wajh in Arabic and translation
Focus on the phrase 'ليس في مقدور الله تعالى سوى هذا' and the conclusion that Mu'tazilites are guilty of 'جهل عظيم'.
Notice the parallel structure: the Mu'tazilites' own principle is turned against them.
2
Analyze the logical chain
Nasafi argues: (1) In the scenario of the known unbeliever, only corruption (إفساد) is possible. (2) Therefore, al-aslah (الأصلح) is not in God's power. (3) The Mu'tazilites obligate God to do al-aslah, i.e., something He cannot do. (4) This contradicts their own rule that God does not oblige a soul beyond its capacity.
3
Appreciate the rhetorical force
Nasafi calls this 'great ignorance' because the Mu'tazilites fail to see the inconsistency in their own position. They impose an impossible duty on God while holding a principle that forbids such imposition.
This argument is both logical and polemical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever expected God to act according to your definition of 'what is best' for you, only to be disappointed? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider your assumptions about what God 'must' do? Will you repent from such demands and submit to His wisdom, even when you don't understand?
?
The Mu'tazilites held a noble principle about God not overburdening His servants, yet they violated it by overburdening God Himself. Where in your own life do you hold others to a standard that you yourself do not meet regarding expectations from God? What step will you take today to align your expectations of God with His revealed attributes, not your own reasoning?
?
Nasafi calls the Mu'tazilite position 'great ignorance.' Is there an area of theology or daily life where you have unknowingly assumed God must fulfill your idea of what is fair or beneficial? How can you actively submit to His power and wisdom, even when His decree seems 'corrupting' to you? Write down one specific situation and a prayer of surrender.

Nasafi's Proof Against Mu'tazilites: Denying Better Welfare Implies Finite Power
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazilites by deducing finitude of God's power from their claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi proves that the Mu'tazilite view entails limiting God's power, which constitutes kufr.

Core Idea
Denying God the ability to do better for the disbeliever implies God's power is finite, which is kufr.

Imam Nasafi presents a concise logical argument against the Mu'tazilite position on divine power. He shows that their claim that God cannot do anything better for the disbeliever necessarily means God's powers are limited. This short passage uses a direct implication to demonstrate that the Mu'tazilite view leads to an unacceptable conclusion about Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: the proof begins with 'mimmā yadullu' (among what indicates).
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim being refuted
The passage says the claim is that there is nothing in God's power that relates to the welfare (ṣalāḥ) of disbelievers and sinners beyond what He has actually done. Find this part in the text.
The claim denies that God could have done better for them.
3
Follow the logical implication
Nasafi argues that this claim necessarily implies that God's powers are finite (tanāhī maqdūrāt Allāh). Trace how the denial of further possibilities leads to a limit on power.
If there is no possible welfare beyond what was done, then God's power cannot go beyond what was done.
4
Understand why this conclusion is kufr
In Islamic theology, affirming the finitude of God's power is kufr because it contradicts God's absolute omnipotence. Nasafi therefore concludes that the Mu'tazilite view is invalid.
5
Reflect on the type of argument
Notice that this is a logical deduction (istidlāl) from the opponent's premise to an unacceptable conclusion. It does not directly accuse the Mu'tazilites of saying God is finite, but shows that their view leads there.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على بطلان قول المعتزلة : إن القول بأن ليس في مقدور الله تعالى شيء يتعلق به صلاح الكافر والعاصي وراء ما فعل بكل واحد من الكفرة والعصاة قول بتناهي مقدورات الله تعالى والقول به كفر
And among what indicates the invalidity of the Mu'tazilites' statement is that the statement that there is not in the power of God Almighty anything related to the welfare of the disbeliever and the sinner beyond what He has done with each of the disbelievers and sinners is a statement asserting the finitude of God's powers, and making that statement is kufr.
Source pages p. 1009

Commentary
This passage provides a single, direct proof (دليل) that the Mu'tazilite position is invalid. The proof is: The Mu'tazilites claim that God does not have in His power anything that could bring about the welfare (صلاح) of the disbeliever or the sinner beyond what He has actually done to each of them. But this claim means that God's powers (مقدورات) are finite; that there is a limit to what He can do. To say that God's powers are finite (تناهي) is kufr (كفر). Therefore, the Mu'tazilite view leads to kufr and is invalid.

Key Terms
مقدورات
تناهي
كفر
صلاح
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Nasafi is accusing Mu'tazilites of directly saying God is finite.
Correction: Nasafi's argument is a logical deduction: if you hold the Mu'tazilite view, it necessarily implies God's power is finite, which is kufr. The accusation is of the implication, not the explicit statement.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does the Mu'tazilite claim that God cannot do anything better for the disbeliever imply?
A) God's mercy is limited.
B) God's justice is unlimited.
C) God's powers are finite.
D) God's knowledge is incomplete.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's argument shows that denying God the power to do better for the disbeliever logically entails the finitude of God's power, which is kufr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that God cannot change a person's heart because they have done too much evil? How does Nasafi's argument challenge that assumption and what does it demand of your faith in God's power?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly subtle theological claim can imply finitude of God's power. Examine your own speech: Are there phrases you use that unintentionally restrict what God can do? Will you commit to replacing them with words that affirm His absolute omnipotence?
?
If you truly believe that God's power is unlimited, how should that transform your prayers for those who are lost? Do you pray with the expectation that God can bring about their guidance, or have you implicitly accepted that some are beyond hope? What will you change today?

Mu'tazilite Objection and Maturidi Refutation on Divine Ability
Nasafi reproduces the Mu'tazilite objection and gives his refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that God's power is unlimited regarding what is beneficial, and Nasafi's logical refutation showing that this leads to either admitting prevention of the most beneficial or attributing inability to God.

Core Idea
God's power is perfect; the Mu'tazilite view implies a contradiction.

In this passage, Nasafi reproduces an objection from the Mu'tazilite scholar al-Ka'bi, who argues that God's power contains infinite possibilities for human benefit and He gives each person exactly what is most beneficial. Nasafi then presents a powerful dilemma to expose the logical flaw in their position. By following his argument, you will see how early Maturidi theologians defended divine omnipotence against rival rationalist positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite objection
Read the first part of the passage where Nasafi quotes al-Ka'bi. Notice the claim that God has unlimited beneficial acts in His power and gives each person the most beneficial.
This sets up the view Nasafi will refute.
2
Identify the dilemma
Nasafi asks: if God has more beneficial acts, would giving them cause a disbeliever to believe? He then examines two possible answers.
The dilemma forces the opponent into a logical corner.
3
Analyze the first branch
If the opponent says 'yes', then God did not give that more beneficial act, so He prevented the most beneficial. That contradicts their claim.
4
Analyze the second branch
If the opponent says 'no', then God lacks power over that act, which attributes incapacity to God.
5
Consider the analogies
Nasafi reinforces with analogies about a grace (lutf) that would cause belief and about movement. These show that denying power over what is known not to happen also implies incapacity.
The analogies generalize the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: نحن لا نقول لما في مقدوره نهاية بل نزعم أنه ليس لما عند الله مما فيه الصلاح غاية ولا نهاية، وأن في سلطانه وقدرته وخزائن رحمته من أمثال ما فعل هو أصلح لهم مما لا غاية له ولا نهاية، والله جل ذكره إنما مما هو بهم يفعل بهم من ذلك ويعطيهم منه في كل وقت مقدار حاجتهم وما يعلم أنه أصلح لهم وأدعى إلى الطاعة وأزجر عن المعصية، ذكرت السؤال بلفظ الكعبي وهو عندهم إمام أهل الأرض ليعلم المتأمل تمويههم في كل ما يتكلمون به، وزوغانهم عن محز الكلام والاشتغال بالتلبيس على العوام. فأقول في جوابه وبالله التوفيق -: إن كان في قدرة الله تعالى وخزائن رحمته أمثال ما فعل من الأصلح فما قولك لو فعل ذلك في الحال بهذا الكافر هل يؤمن؟ فإن قال : نعم. فقد أقر بمنع الأصلح إذ لم يفعل ذلك كله ليؤمن، وإن قال : لا قيل فإذا لا قدرة له على ما به لو آمن من هذا العبد فحصل له صلاحه، فإذا السؤال أنه هل يقدر أن يفعل به في الوقت أصلح من هذا الذي فعل أم لا يصفه بالقدرة عليه؟ فينبغي أن يجيب عن هذا الحرف أو يعترض عليه، وإذا لم يكن عندك في مقدوره أن يفعل به في الوقت أصلح من هذا فهذا هو آية النفاد والعجز عن تحصيل المراد. ثم يقال له : أليس من زعم أن الله تعالى يقدر أن يفعل في الوقت صلاحًا هو أصلح الأشياء للإنسان ولا يوصف بالقدرة على مثله يكون معجزا ربه؟ فلا بد من بلي. فقيل له: فإنك وإن أثبت قدرة على أمثال ما فعل فإذا لم تصفه بالقدرة على ما هو أصلح له أصلح له من هذا وصفته بالعجز أيضا ، ثم يقال له أليس أن من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على لطف يؤمن به من علم أنه يؤمن واصفًا له بالعجز؟ فإذا قال نعم قيل فكذا من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على لطف يؤمن به من علم أنه لا يؤمن به يكون واصفا له بالعجز كما أن من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على تحريك من يعلم أنه لا يحركه يكون واصفًا له بالعجز كمن لا يصفه بالقدرة على تحريك
For if they say: We do not say that there is an end to what is in His power, rather we claim that there is no limit or end to what God has of what is beneficial, and that in His sovereignty, power, and treasuries of mercy there are examples of what He has done that are more beneficial for them without limit or end, and God, exalted be His mention, only does to them from that and gives them from it at every time the amount of their need and what He knows is most beneficial for them and most conducive to obedience and most deterrent from disobedience. I have mentioned the question in the wording of al-Ka'bi, who is according to them the imam of the people of the earth, so that the ponderer may know their deception in everything they speak, and their deviation from the core of discourse and their occupation with obfuscation upon the common people. So I say in answer to him, and with God is success: If in the power of God Almighty and the treasuries of His mercy there are examples of what He has done of the most beneficial, then what do you say: if He did that immediately to this disbeliever, would he believe? If he says: Yes, then he has admitted the prevention of the most beneficial since He did not do all of that so that he would believe. And if he says: No, then it is said: So He has no power over that by which this servant would believe and thus his welfare would be attained. So the question is: does He have the power to do what is more beneficial for him at the time than this which He did, or do you not describe Him as having power over it? He should answer this point or object to it. And if according to you there is not in His power to do what is more beneficial for him at the time than this, then this is the sign of exhaustion and incapacity to attain the desired. Then it is said to him: Is it not that whoever claims that God Almighty is able to do at the time a benefit that is the most beneficial of things for man, yet does not describe Him as having power over the like of it, would be attributing inability to his Lord? So he must say yes. So it is said to him: For even if you affirm power over examples of what He has done, if you do not describe Him as having power over what is more beneficial for him than this, you also describe Him with incapacity. Then it is said to him: Is it not that whoever does not describe God Almighty with the power over a grace (lutf) by which one would believe whom He knows will believe, is describing Him with incapacity? So if he says yes, it is said: Likewise, whoever does not describe God Almighty with the power over a grace by which one would believe whom He knows will not believe, is describing Him with incapacity, just as whoever does not describe God Almighty with the power to move whom He knows He will not move is describing Him with incapacity, like one who does not describe Him as having power to move.
Source pages p. 1009

Commentary
This passage contains an objection from the Mu'tazilites and Nasafi's response. The Mu'tazilites (quoting al-Ka'bi) object that they do not claim God's power has a limit; rather, they say God has infinite resources of what is beneficial (صلاح) and He gives each person the amount that is most beneficial for them according to their need. Nasafi states he reproduces the objection verbatim from al-Ka'bi to show their deception. Then he gives his response: He poses a dilemma. If God has in His power examples of what is more beneficial than what He has done, then ask: if God were to give that immediately to the disbeliever, would the disbeliever believe? If the opponent says 'yes', then he has admitted that God prevented the most beneficial (since He did not give it and the disbeliever did not believe). If the opponent says 'no', then God does not have the power to cause belief in that servant, so He cannot achieve the servant's welfare. So either way, the opponent must either affirm that God could have done more beneficial but did not (which contradicts their claim) or admit that God lacks the power to do so (which is attributing inability to God). Nasafi then reinforces with analogies: anyone who denies that God has power over a grace (لطف) that would cause belief in one whom He knows will believe is attributing inability to God; similarly, denying power over a grace for one whom He knows will not believe also attributes inability. The analogy of movement is given: denying power to move what one knows He will not move also attributes inability.

Key Terms
أصلح
صلاح
لطف
عجز
قدرة
الكعبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Maturidi response denies that God can cause belief in the disbeliever.
Correction: Actually, Nasafi argues that the power exists; the point is about the logical consequences of the Mu'tazilite position.

Checkpoint
In Nasafi's response, if the Mu'tazilite says that God has the power to do more beneficial but does not, then what does Nasafi conclude?
A) The Mu'tazilite has admitted that God prevented the most beneficial.
B) The Mu'tazilite has affirmed God's perfect power.
C) The Mu'tazilite has proven God's justice.
D) The Mu'tazilite has denied God's mercy.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite position by showing that either God could have given more beneficial but did not (implying prevention) or He lacks power over it (implying incapacity), both of which are unacceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle with accepting God's decree—especially when you see someone you love not guided—do you silently doubt God's power, thinking He could have done more if He wanted? How does this lesson's refutation of 'prevention of the most beneficial' push you to trust that God's wisdom and power are perfectly matched?
?
Have you ever felt that God could have given you a better situation in life—more health, wealth, or ease—and wondered why He didn't? This lesson shows that such thinking either implies God withholds the best or is incapable. Which of those two does your heart lean toward in moments of complaint? Are you ready to repent of that?
?
The Mu'tazilite said God gives each person exactly what is 'most beneficial' for them. If you truly believe that, does your contentment with your own life—and with the lot of others—reflect that belief? Or do you still wish for changes that contradict His perfect choice? Ask yourself: Can you genuinely say, 'God gave me what is best' without any reservation?

First Refutation of the Mu'tazila Analogy
Nasafi's first refutation of the Mu'tazila's shubha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's analogy from the witnessed to the unseen is invalid if equality between the two realms is not established.

Core Idea
Analogy without proof of equality is invalid.

The Mu'tazila argue that a wise commander would never prevent his subject from obtaining the means to obey. But this argument is based on human experience. Imam Nasafi begins his refutation by questioning whether it is valid to apply a rule from our world to God's actions without a proven connection. This lesson focuses on that first, fundamental critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author presents the Mu'tazila claim and then his response.
The passage is cut off; the first aspect is complete.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Find the Mu'tazila's shubha: that a wise commander who commands and desires obedience will not prevent the means to obey. Understand the logic.
3
Understand the first refutation
The author says the Mu'tazila argued from the witnessed realm (shahid) without establishing equality (taswiya) with the unseen (gha'ib). Why is this a problem?
The author demands a justification for the analogy.
4
Reflect on the necessity of proof
Ask yourself: Can we simply transfer a rule from human experience to Allah without evidence? The first refutation shows this is insufficient.
This connects to the broader method of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما اعتمدوا عليه من الشبه فنقول قولهم إنا وجدنا الحكيم إذا كان أمرًا بطاعة محبًا لها مريدًا فلن يجوز أن يمنع المأمور ما يصل به إلى طاعته إلى آخر ما حكينا. كلام باطل من وجوه. أحدها: أنه استدل على هذا بالشاهد من غير إثبات التسوية فيقال له: هب أن الأمر في الشاهد كان على ما زعمت، ولكن لم ينبغي أن يكون الأمر كذلك في الغائب؟ وبأي معنى تجمع بينهما ؟ فإن زعمت أن كل ما ثبت في الشاهد كان
As for what they rely on from specious arguments, we say: Their statement: 'We find that the wise person, when he commands an act of obedience, loves it and wills it, it is not permissible for him to prevent the commanded person from that which would enable him to reach his obedience' up to the end of what we narrated. This is false speech from several aspects. First: That he argued this by the witnessed realm without establishing equality. So it is said to him: Grant that the matter in the witnessed realm is as you claimed, but why should it be the same in the unseen realm? And by what meaning do you assemble between them? For if you claim that everything established in the witnessed realm was
Source pages p. 1017

Commentary
This passage is the start of a refutation of the Mu'tazila's shubha (specious argument) based on analogy from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib). The Mu'tazila claim that a wise commander who commands obedience and desires it will not prevent the commanded person from obtaining the means to obey. The author declares this argument false from multiple aspects. He then presents the first aspect: the Mu'tazila argue from the witnessed realm without establishing the equality (taswiya) between the witnessed and the unseen. The author demands: even if the premise holds in the witnessed, why must it hold in the unseen? What valid connection unites them? The text is cut off, but the argument continues in the original source.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that what is true for human commanders is automatically true for Allah.
Correction: The passage shows that such an assumption is invalid without establishing equality between the witnessed and unseen realms.

The Mu'tazila's analogy from the witnessed to the unseen is invalid unless equality (taswiya) is proven between the two realms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson exposes the danger of using human analogies to judge Allah. Is there an area of your faith where you have subtly imposed your own sense of fairness onto Allah's decrees? What step will you take today to surrender that assumption to revelation?

The Absurd Consequence of the Mu'tazilite Principle
Al-Nasafi shows the absurd consequence of the Mu'tazilite position regarding God's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi uses reductio ad absurdum to refute the Mu'tazilite principle that God must always do what is most beneficial.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite principle implies that prophets' lives are harmful, a consequence no believer can accept.

Al-Nasafi now exposes a shocking implication of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aṣlaḥ (the most beneficial). If God were compelled to always choose the best for His servants, then the prolonged life of prophets would be detrimental, and even the existence of Satan would be preferable. This passage demonstrates the logical absurdity of the Mu'tazilite view, forcing the reader to recognize its incompatibility with sound theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once with the translation. Let the full argument settle before analyzing.
Notice how al-Nasafi uses a hypothetical scenario to draw out the consequence.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
The passage applies the Mu'tazilite principle to the Prophet Muhammad and then to all messengers. What is the logical outcome?
It leads to the conclusion that their prolonged existence is harmful.
3
Contrast with Iblis
Al-Nasafi contrasts the righteous with Iblis and the wicked. Why is this contrast so powerful?
It inverts moral intuition, showing the principle leads to a repugnant result.
4
Reflect on the author's response
Notice how al-Nasafi condemns the statement and invokes God's opposition. Why is this emotional appeal part of the argument?
It reinforces that no believer can accept such a conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مآل مذهبهم إلى أن الله تعالى لو بقى محمدًا عليه السلام لمحة بصر وراء الساعة التي قبض روحه فيها لكفر الله تعالى وعصاه وكان قبض روحه في تلك الساعة أصلح له، وكذا في كل رسول ونبي وولي وصديق، فكان بقاء الرسل والأخيار مفسدة لهم وللخلق وبقاء إبليس والأشرار أصلح لهم وللخلق، وهذا قول لا يحتمل قلب مؤمن سماعه لفظاعته في نفسه وبشاعته في ذاته، والله خصيم من هذا قوله في رسله وأنبيائه المصطفين الأخيار وأوليائه وعباده الأبرار، ونسأل الله العصمة من الضلال والتمادي في الغي والجهالة.
Then the consequence of their doctrine is that if God had kept Muhammad, peace be upon him, for the twinkling of an eye beyond the hour in which He took his soul, then God would have disbelieved and disobeyed Him, and taking his soul at that hour was more beneficial for him. And likewise for every messenger, prophet, saint, and truthful person. So the continued existence of messengers and the righteous is harmful for them and for creation, and the continued existence of Iblis and the wicked is more beneficial for them and for creation. This is a statement that no believer's heart can bear to hear, because of its heinousness in itself and its ugliness in its essence. And God is the opponent of whoever says this about His messengers, chosen prophets, righteous saints, and pious servants. And we ask God for protection from misguidance and persistence in error and ignorance.
Source pages p. 1017

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum of the Mu'tazilite principle that God must always do what is most beneficial for His servants. Al-Nasafi argues that if this principle were true, then it would imply that God should have taken the Prophet Muhammad's soul earlier, because keeping him alive longer would have been less beneficial—indeed, it would lead to kufr (disbelief) for him. The same applies to all messengers, prophets, saints, and truthful people: their continued existence becomes harmful, while the continued existence of Iblis (Satan) and the wicked becomes more beneficial. This conclusion is so repugnant that no believer can accept it. Al-Nasafi then invokes divine opposition to such a view and concludes with a prayer for guidance.

Key Terms
مآل
مذهب
أصلح
مفسدة
رسول
ولي
صديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites did not intend this consequence, but they argued that God's actions are always the most beneficial.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that even if unintended, this consequence necessarily follows from their principle, proving the principle false.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does al-Nasafi draw from the Mu'tazilite principle that God must always do what is most beneficial?
A) Prophets are the most sinful of people.
B) Prolonging the lives of prophets is harmful to them.
C) God is unjust in His actions.
D) Humans have no free will.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi demonstrates that the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aṣlaḥ leads to the absurd conclusion that the lives of prophets are harmful and the existence of Iblis is beneficial, thus refuting the principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God's wisdom surpasses your understanding, how does the Mu'tazilite claim that God must always do what is 'most beneficial' undermine your trust in His plan? What specific area of your life do you need to surrender to God's will rather than demanding what seems best to you?
?
The passage concludes that no believer's heart can bear the heinous conclusion of the Mu'tazilite principle. Is there any belief or principle you hold that, if logically extended, would lead to a conclusion you find repugnant? What is it, and are you willing to reexamine it today?
?
Al-Nasafi prays for protection from misguidance and persistence in error. In what area of your faith are you most vulnerable to being misled by seemingly rational principles that contradict revelation? How will you actively seek God's protection from that misguidance starting today?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
5 min
Refuting the Unqualified Analogy between Seen and Unseen
Al-Nasafi presents the first refutation: the analogy between shahid and gha'ib is invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite argument from analogy between the seen (shahid) and unseen (gha'ib) is invalid without establishing equivalence (taswiya).

Core Idea
Analogy without a shared underlying cause is false in kalam.

Al-Nasafi begins his refutation by identifying the first flaw: the Mu'tazilites draw an analogy between human commanders and God without justification. He provides two concrete examples—the cuckold and the creation of bodies—to show that the seen world operates differently from the unseen. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Nasafi first summarizes the Mu'tazilite argument, then presents his first criticism.
Pay attention to the phrase 'من غير إثبات التسوية'—the key flaw.
2
Identify the two examples
The passage gives two examples (the cuckold and the creation of bodies) to show that analogies between shahid and gha'ib fail. Find both and understand why each breaks the analogy.
The first example shows that what is blameworthy in the seen need not be blameworthy in the unseen. The second shows that the seen lacks the power to create bodies.
3
Understand the conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that if the Mu'tazilites insist on the analogy, they end up contradicting themselves or falling into heresy. Grasp how this undermines their argument.
