⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة أخرى للمعتزلة والرد عليها فأما ما اعتمدوا عليه من الشبه فنقول قولهم إنا وجدنا الحكيم إذا كان أمرًا بطاعة محبًا لها مريدًا فلن يجوز أن يمنع المأمور ما يصل به إلى طاعته إلى آخر ما حكينا. كلام باطل من وجوه. أحدها: أنه استدل على هذا بالشاهد من غير إثبات التسوية فيقال له: هب أن الأمر في الشاهد كان على ما زعمت، ولكن لم ينبغي أن يكون الأمر كذلك في الغائب؟ وبأي معنى تجمع بينهما؟ فإن زعمت أن كل ما ثبت في الشاهد كان إثباته في الغائب واجبًا من غير اعتبار المعنى، فذلك باطل وقد بينا قبل هذا أن من رأى عبده يزني بأمته فلم يمنع مع القدرة على المنع بل اشتغل بتهيئة أسباب ذلك كان ديوثًا سفيها وفي الغائب الأمر بخلافه، وكذا في الشاهد لا قدرة لفاعل ما على إيجاد الأجسام وإنما يقدر الفاعل في الشاهد على بعض الأعراض وعندكم لا يقدر أيضا إلا على إثبات الأفعال في محال هذه الأجسام فلو قسم الغائب على الشاهد لبطل القول بخلق الأجسام وهو قول الدهرية. وإن أثبتم ذلك في الغائب وإن امتنع إثبات مثله في الشاهد أبطلتم كلامكم. ثم يقال: على أي حكيم يجب ما ذكرت من بذل جميع ما في مقدوره لمن دعاه إلى طاعته وطلب رجوعه عن معصيته؟ أعلى حكيم محتاج إلى طاعة المطيع، ورجوع العدو عن العداوة متعذر بكثرة أعوانه وأنصاره وتكاتف حزبه وأوليائه ذليل عند كثرة أعدائه؟ أم على حكيم مستغن عن طاعة غيره إياه عزيز في ذاته قوي في سلطانه لا يضعفه كثرة الأعداء ولا يقويه كثرة الأولياء؟ فإن قالوا بالأول فهو مسلم ولكن لا وجه إلى تعديته إلى الغائب. وإن قالوا بالثاني فهو ممنوع ثم نقول لهم: لو كان الأمر على ما ذكرتم أن الأمر كان مريدًا لطاعة المأمور محبًا لها، وكذا لرجوع العدو لكان ما قلت مسلمًا ولكان يعطي لا محالة ويبذل ما يحتاج إليه المأمور للامتثال ولكن لم قلتم أن البار جل
Another objection of the Mu'tazila and the refutation of it: As for what they rely on of the objections, we say: their statement, 'We found that the wise person, when he commands obedience, loves it and desires it, it is not permissible for him to withhold from the commanded what enables him to perform his obedience,' up to what we related. This is false speech from several aspects. One of them: it deduces this from the seen without establishing equivalence. It is said to him: suppose that the matter in the seen was as you claim, but why should the matter be thus in the unseen? And by what meaning do you combine between them? If you claim that everything established in the seen must be established in the unseen without considering the meaning, that is false. And we have shown before this that whoever sees his slave committing adultery with his slave girl and does not prevent him despite ability to prevent, but rather busies himself with preparing the means for that, is a cuckold and foolish. And in the unseen the matter is contrary to that. Likewise, in the seen, no agent has power to create bodies, but rather the agent in the seen is only capable of some accidents, and according to you he is also only capable of creating actions in the loci of these bodies. So if the unseen is measured by the seen, the statement of creation of bodies would be invalid, which is the statement of the materialists. And if you establish that in the unseen, and the establishment of its like in the seen is impossible, you invalidate your speech. Then it is said: upon which wise person does what you mentioned become obligatory, i.e., expending all that is in his power to one who calls him to his obedience and seeks his return from his disobedience? Is it upon a wise person who is in need of the obedience of the obedient, and the return of the enemy from enmity is made difficult by the large number of his supporters and helpers and the cohesion of his party and allies, and he is humiliated by the large number of his enemies? Or upon a wise person who is independent of the obedience of others to him, mighty in His essence, strong in His authority, whom the abundance of enemies does not weaken nor the abundance of allies strengthen? If they say the first, it is conceded, but there is no way to extend it to the unseen. And if they say the second, it is denied. Then we say to them: if the matter were as you mentioned, that the commander desires the obedience of the commanded and loves it, and likewise the return of the enemy, then what you said would be conceded, and he would indeed give and expend what the commanded needs for compliance. But why do you say that the Creator (al-Barr)...
Source pages p. 1017

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazilite shubha (objection). The Mu'tazilites argue that a wise commander who commands obedience and desires it must provide the means for that obedience; otherwise, he would be acting foolishly. Al-Nasafi responds by pointing out multiple flaws. First, the argument relies on an unqualified analogy between the seen world (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib). He gives two examples: a man who sees his slave committing adultery and does nothing is considered a cuckold (dayyuth), but this does not apply to God; also, in the seen world no agent can create bodies, only accidents, whereas God creates bodies. So the analogy fails. Second, he asks which kind of wise person the obligation applies to: a needy wise person who depends on others, or a self-sufficient wise person? If the Mu'tazilites say the first, the analogy cannot be extended to God; if they say the second, their premise is denied because a self-sufficient wise person is not obligated in the same way. Third, even if we concede the premise, the Mu'tazilites still have not proven that God withholds means; in fact, God provides all necessary guidance, but His wisdom is not subject to human expectations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites believed that any analogy between shahid and gha'ib is valid without identifying a common cause (illa).
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that analogies require a shared meaning; otherwise, they lead to absurdities like the cuckold example or materialism.

Checkpoint
Which example does al-Nasafi use to show that judgments in the seen do not necessarily apply to the unseen?
A) A king rewarding obedience
B) A man who sees his slave committing adultery and does nothing
C) A father teaching his son
D) A judge executing a criminal

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite argument fails because it relies on an unqualified analogy between the seen and unseen, which al-Nasafi refutes with counterexamples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself arguing with someone about God's actions, do you ever unknowingly use human standards without establishing that they apply to Him? How can you train yourself to recognize this error before speaking?
?
This lesson shows that even seemingly logical arguments can fail if they rely on unexamined analogies. In your own understanding of God, which analogy have you held that, upon reflection, might be imposing human limitations on the Divine? Will you commit to re-examining it today?
?
Al-Nasafi's refutation forces us to admit that we cannot simply assume God should act like a wise human. Are you willing to accept that God's wisdom may be contrary to your expectations? If so, how will you adjust your worship and trust in Him when His decree appears to conflict with your sense of justice?
